Characterization of potentially geo-eective
events near Earth orbit and estimation of the
associated risk based on solar wind magnetic
eld data
Naja Nørgaard Nielsen, s134373
Supervisor: Susanne Vennerstrøm, Senior Scientist, DTU Space
16th of September 2018
Master thesis
==PAGE:4==
 
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This study aims at developing a program, which can detect, analyse, categorise, and estimate risk of dis-
turbances in the solar wind based solemnly on magnetic eld data. The program must be able to analyse
data in real-time, in order to provide warnings if a critical situation occur. The automatised detection
can improve forecasting in general, and this method is more robust to highly elevated conditions in the
solar wind, since magnetometers are less sensitive compared to plasma measurement instruments. The
objective is to prove that it is possible to categorise the disturbances with a reasonable success rate, only
from magnetic features.
The program is developed using OMNIweb data, where two score tables for ICMEs and SIR respectively are
introduced. They consist of relevant criteria each connected to three dierent thresholds and their respec-
tive score points. The criteria are chosen based on literature and investigations of existing ICME and SIR
catalogues to distinguish between features in the disturbances. Then the ve categories "ICME","ICME-
like", "SIR", "SIR-like" and "unidentied" are presented based on the score of each disturbance relative
to the maximum possible score. The performance is evaluated by matching the categorised events to the
relevant catalogues.
The results of the OMNIweb data based development test show a better detection rate for the test data
from 1998-2008 compared to the validation data from 2009-2014, which is assumed to be caused by the
variations during the solar cycle. For the test period ≈70% of the disturbances are categorised, whereas
it is ≈60% for the validation period. The percentage of disturbances, which are categorised and matched
in the right catalogue is between 75% −93% for the test period and 50% −75% for the validation period.
This indicates a general lower detection rate assumed to be caused by changes during the solar cycle. A
more dynamic way of determining the threshold values are concluded to be essential to improving the
performance of the program. The criteria must also be re-evaluated to better target non-MC ICMEs and
minimise the number of "unidentied" events.
The risk assessment is based on the linear relation between Bzmin and Dstmin that shows promising re-
sults, but the warnings can with advantage be expanded to include other indices and parameters such as
shocks and duration of negative Bz intervals.
Implementation of the DSCOVR real-time data shows that the detection, analysis, categorisation and risk
assessment works with fairly good results. The categorisation is right for a large part of the events, as
soon as the whole disturbance is visible in the analysis. The risk assessment has some tendencies of over-
estimating the minimum of the Dst index, when compared to the ocial measurement, but the method is
simple and eective.

Introduction
In 2017, the Danish Emergency Management Agency (Beredskabsstyrelsen) published a new list of threats
against Denmark, for which the government and other relevant parties need to prepare and make precau-
tions in case of impacts. A new addition to the list was "Space weather" [22]. Space weather and more
precisely the geomagnetic storms caused by space weather phenomena have increased their importance as
the society becomes more and more depended on modern technology. The threat, that geomagnetic storms
possess towards satellite communication, global navigation satellite systems, GNSS, electrical power grid
and more, is stronger now that the technology is included in a wide range of applications [23]. This project
will address the challenge of automatically detecting space weather phenomena and estimating the risk
of the impact by only using magnetic eld data. It has proven very relevant to do real-time detection of
disturbances based on magnetic eld data only to strengthening the redundancy of automated solar wind
observations. Before the exact aim of the project is presented, it is necessary to know the background of
space weather.
Our nearest star, the vital Sun is a complex structure of plasma, magnetic elds and gravitational forces.
The solar energy is vital to the life on Earth, but some interactions can challenge the natural protection
system of Earth's magnetic eld surrounding us. Mankind has observed the Sun for many years, but the
complete understanding of the Sun and its behaviour is still a goal for the future. Many questions are
unanswered and our observations only go back an instant compared to the life time of the Sun. Even
then many important discoveries have been made, and space weather is one of the modern age's most
important new areas of interest. Since the beginning of space weather research, many new things have
been discovered, and the awareness from society motivates the scientists to solve the puzzles remaining
[24].
The short explanation of space weather is the constant interaction between the solar wind streaming contin-
uously from the Sun and the dynamic magnetosphere of a planet. It constantly emits plasma in a steady
stream called the solar wind. Sometimes large energy eruptions from the atmosphere of the Sun eject
clouds of plasma, or the velocity of the solar wind increases signicantly and creates compressed regions
following the rotation of the Sun. The best prove for space weather being a far-reaching phenomenon is
that aurora has been observed at dierent planets in the solar system e.g. Jupiter and Saturn [25]. Aurora
is the most easily recognised consequences of space weather. Since the magnetic eld and the atmosphere
of each planet is dierent, it requires separate studies to understand the interaction. This project will
focus on space weather on Earth, since it is here where impacts on the society are relevant and present.
If future space missions are to travel to other planets or asteroids space weather scientists will have to
work on new models and forecasting tools reaching further out in the solar system. This means that the
more we learn on Earth, the better suited we are to protect astronauts going further away.
The magnetic eld of the Earth protects humans from the dangerous radiation and particle streams coming
from the Sun and the interstellar space. The DNA can be damaged by exposure to charged particles and it
can lead to cancer [26]. Astronauts aboard the space station are partly protected by the Earth's magnetic
eld whereas radiation hardening of the outer shell of the space-vehicle is needed too. If the International
Space Station (ISS) is hit head on by a strong geomagnetic storm, the crew is asked to enter a better
protected capsule or if extreme, abandon the ISS, since vital functions can also sustain damage and the
astronauts' life can be at stake. This has not been necessary yet. For future long-distance space missions,
protection of the astronauts is very important [27].
==PAGE:18==
2
Introduction
A very signicant moment in the history of space weather occurred on the 1st of September 1859, where
Richard C. Carrington and Richard Hodgson independently of one another observed the rst known solar
eruption of a are that was accompanied by a coronal mass ejection. 17 hours later an intense geomag-
netic storm encountered Earth. The society back then was also inicted by the event, where some of
the telegraph line between Europe and the United States broke down or telegraphs kept working even
though the power was cut. Spectacular aurora was also observed at much lower latitudes than previously
seen e.g. in Caribbean. The light was so intense that some mine workers in the Rocky mountains woke
up and started their breakfast. The uctuations in the geomagnetic activity on Earth observed by the
Kew Observatory in London led R. Carrington to conclude that the event observed on the Sun must have
caused the geomagnetic disturbance and thereby established a solar-terrestrial link. This giant solar erup-
tion and the following geomagnetic storm are famous because it is the largest impact of a space weather
phenomenon known from modern time. It is known as the Carrington event [28]. A collaboration between
researchers from Lloyd's of London and AER (Atmopsheric and Environmental Research) in the United
States estimated that an event of the size would cost the American society 0.6 to 2.6 trillion dollars to
restore from today [28].
The threat of space weather is a bit obscure, since extremely powerful impacts like the Carrington event
are known from history, but they are very rare and non-periodic, similar to the giant earthquakes like in
the Indian ocean near Sumatra in December 2004. New research has also shown that just before year 1000,
two extremely powerful geomagnetic storms hit Earth within 300 years. Their strength is estimated to be
ve times that of the Carrington event [29]. The consequences of a geomagnetic storm like this hitting
tomorrow are almost unimaginable.
The worst case scenario is important when consequences of impact are considered, but it is dicult to
prepare for something, which might not happen in another 100 years. The motivation from society is often
bound to challenges, which are close to themselves. Therefore, it is important to emphasize that the geo-
magnetic storms happening in every decade can cause great impact too and require attention, research and
daily monitoring. Some events in the past have shown this and it highlighted some of the potential threats.
In 1989 the region Quebec in Canada got a taste of one of the most problematic consequences. In 12
hours the whole region involving 6 million people experienced a power outage because one transformer
was destroyed by geomagnetically induced currents. The estimated cost of this incident was 10 million US
dollars from the power station alone [27]. The total cost has been estimated to around 6 billion US dollars
[30]. In worst case scenarios this could happen for large areas at the higher latitudes on the northern and
southern hemisphere. The consequences in modern society would be severe, since emergency systems are
not build for these impacts and many applications depend on continuous supply of power. The power
grids in many countries are also connected, which means the damage can spread quickly [31]. Also spare
parts for the transformers are limited. A report estimated that a large-scale black-out could aect 130
million people and expose more than 350 transformer stations to permanent damage [32]. It will take
months to rebuild completely and it will be critical for institutions like hospitals [27]. This, along with the
disturbance of satellite communication signals, is seen as the high priority dangers.
The satellite communication is based on electromagnetic signals and when the state of the atmosphere
changes due to large amount of charged particles entering the lower levels of the atmosphere, the propa-
gation changes. It means signals can get lost or transmitted wrongly. Satellite communication is involved
in parts of society, which is more indirect such as the international stock markets. If a geomagnetic storm
interferes with the trading, it can cause some very chaotic situations, which might be very serious eco-
nomically for companies and organisations. For GNSS it is critical since the position given can be wrong.
Errors up to 100 meters have been measured, which is 100 times worse than the normal accuracy [27].
If the aected equipment is used to control a plane, which is landing or a ship manoeuvring in dicult
territory then errors like this can be fatal. As autonomous vehicles are entering society, the accuracy of
the navigation equipment is very important and this emphasises the need to accommodate this challenge.
Several examples have been encountered in airports where they had to delay or cancel many departures
due to safety reasons. Sweden has experienced problems e.g. in November 2015, where the Arlanda air-
port near Stockholm among other had to close operations for a few hours [33]. It is very expensive when
airports are closed and the future technology for areas like this must take space weather into account. In
2003 a geomagnetic storm called the Halloween storm destroyed a Japanese satellite Midori 2, designed to
observe the climate, worth 570 million dollars. This is not the rst satellite which have malfunctioned due
to space weather. Many working hours and money are lost, which is a frustrating situation.
==PAGE:19==
3
The consequences of worst case scenarios are frightening and in truth dicult to prevent, but on a daily
basis space weather forecasts serve as an important tool to meet some of the less extreme, but still very im-
portant geomagnetic storms. Spacecraft observatories positioned between Earth and the Sun have greatly
improved the changes of predicting potential impacts. It provides real-time observations, both as images
of the solar surface in dierent wavelengths or measurements of the current solar wind. Today, many of
the analyses are done manually by examining the data everyday and conclude if anything has happened.
Disturbances can be manifested in dierent ways, but in general some parameters will exceed the normal
level. This combined with the images in X-ray and near-visible light and coronagraphs can give forecasters
a pretty good idea of what is going on. The underlying mechanisms that cause some of these events are
still unknown, but as more data is obtained, the hope is to improve the forecasting time and gain a better
knowledge of the driving forces of space weather.
Previous studies have examined the features of the most well known disturbances: Interplanetary Coronal
Mass Ejections (ICMEs) and Stream Interaction Regions (SIRs) and their subgroups. The general features
have been studied in articles such as "Properties of Stream Interactions at one AU during 1995-2004" by
L. Jian [8], and "Near-Earth Interplanetary Coronal Mass Ejections During solar Cycle 23 (1996-2009):
Catalog and Summery of Properties" by I. Richardson and H. Cane [7]. The investigations in both studies
are carried out manually during the mentioned time periods. Specic features known from both type of
disturbances such as shocks have also been investigated e.g. in "Properties and drivers of fast interplanetary
shocks near the orbit of Earth (1995-2013)" by E. K. J. Kilpua [34]. A very important subcategory of
ICMEs was proposed by Burlaga in 1981 [35] and has since been studies heavily in e.g. "Internal structure
of magnetic clouds: Plasma and composition" by B. J. Lynch [36] and "Relationships Among Geomagnetic
Storms, Interplanetary shocks, Magnetic clouds, and Sunspot Number During 1995-2012" by C. Wu [37].
These articles are the foundation for building an automated detection program since they describe the
relevant features of the disturbances.
Other studies have also worked with automated detection programs, but the scope has been dierent. V.
S. Vorotnikov et. al. [38] analysed the possibilities of detecting interplanetary shocks and the same did O.
Kruparova et. al. [39] and M. D. Cash et. al. [40]. The programs are based on relevant theories and models
with high detection rates. Another approach is to use the images of the Sun to detect active regions. The
study by D. Pérez-Suárez et. al. [41] concluded that the process was possible and new spacecraft would
improve the success rate signicantly. This study was done before the Solar Dynamic Observatory was
launched and greatly improved the available image data, but the concept is similar future improvements
of forecasting.
The most similar study to this project is carried out by Susanne Vennerstrøm at DTU and has resulted
in AWARE (Automated WARnings of Earth arrivals) [42], which has been presented to me in person
prior to this project. It uses real-time data to detect and categorise disturbances in the solar wind. This
project is inspired by AWARE, and some functions are similar, since an early idea was for the programs
to supplement each other.
This leads to the aim of this project, which is to build a fully automated program, which can detect,
categorise and perform risk analysis on disturbances in the solar wind. The dierence from AWARE is
that this program will only include magnetic eld data from the solar wind. The program will be developed
using available data from the OMNIweb portal and STEREO data archive. Thereafter, the program must
be transformed to download and analyse real-time data from DSCOVR using the same categorisation and
risk analysis process. The performance must be evaluated by comparing the categorisations to existing
catalogues of events.
The importance of this program is based on the analysed data. Magnetometers are less sensitive to extreme
space weather conditions compared to the instruments measuring the plasma parameters (temperature,
velocity, density, etc.), which mean that fewer data gaps exist. Magnetometers will provide data even
during extreme disturbances, which is very important for forecasting. The purpose is therefore also to
investigate if the categorisation is possible with the limited data. The magnetic signatures of the dierent
types of events will be tested to see if they are signicant enough to distinguish between them. Some
overlap are expected, but it is interesting to investigate how dominating they will be.
This report will explore the process of developing the program, which has been done during a ve month
master thesis at the Technical University of Denmark. At rst the relevant theory is presented which
the categorisation and risk analysis are based on. The most important features of the disturbances are

highlighted along with a description of geoeectiveness and its meaning. The applied data is also presented
along with the specications of relevant instruments.
Then the process during the program development phase is elaborated in close to chronological order.

Theoretical background
Space weather is one of the new area of research within the eld of space science. The importance of
these new discoveries are emphasized, the more we learn about the consequences of severe space weather
conditions and their impacts on Earth.
Space weather can be compared to the normal weather and weather forecasts known from our daily life. The
driving force in both cases is the Sun and the active player is the atmosphere and magnetosphere around
planets, in this case Earth. As the energy exchange changes over time, so does the weather. Even though
weather and space weather are conceptually alike, they do not interact with each other. The movement
and interaction of the solar wind are a complex combination of uid dynamics, electromagnetism, plasma
physics and more, but some central approximations have made it easier to work with and model. The main
theory involved is magnetohydrodynamics, which will be described in the following section. The features
of the solar wind and events, known to be important for space weather, will be described later on. The
impact and possible consequences on Earth will also elaborated to create an understanding of the potential
dangers.
2.1
Magnetohydrodynamics
This section is based on the lectures in the course "Magnetohydrodynamic Phenomena - An Introduction"
PH2037 at Technical University of Munich W2017/8 conducted by Prof. K. Hallatschek
Magnetohydrodynamics is a special case of plasma physics where the theory of charged particle motions
is combined with uid dynamics. To apply this scheme, some assumptions are necessary. The main as-
sumption is that the plasma is an ideal plasma, meaning it is fully ionised, but treated as one uid, and
it has a very high conductivity. The plasma also needs to show quasi-neutrality, where the plasma overall
is neutral, but the charges become clear on smaller scales. The best known ideal plasma is the interstellar
medium, but the theory works well on the solar wind too.
Charged particles in a magnetic eld will move in spiral motion along the eld line called gyration with the
Langmuir radius due to the Lorentz force equalising the centrifugal force. The phenomenon creates a ux
tube. The structure is stable even if the eld lines are bent due to of external forces. It is called frozen-in
ux because the magnetic ux is always the same. It is well known that if the magnetic ux density is
changed due to motion, it will induce a current which will then result in a opposite directed magnetic eld
as described by Lenz's law. Lenz's law states that the induced current makes the enclosed magnetic eld
exactly opposite and equal strength so that the total magnetic eld is then zero [43]. Therefore the overall
ux is unchanged. It is important to note that it is the ux that is unchanged, not the magnetic eld. A
classic example is a ux tube that encloses a group of eld lines. If the side of the cylinder is compressed
by some force, the cross section decreases, but the magnetic eld ux density increases. Away from the
compressed region the ux tube will expand and the magnetic ux density will decrease. The magnetic
ux Φ is constant under the changes. It means that the magnetic ux density, B, is proportional to the
inverse cross section area, A.
B ∼Φ
A ⇒2B ∼Φ
1
2A
At the same time topology, which describes the shape if the magnetic eld lines, is conserved. The only
way to break a ux tube is reconnection where two crossing or closed packed eld lines break to bond in
==PAGE:22==
6
Theoretical background
a new conguration. An example can be seen in Figure 2.2. This is very important for eruptions from
the Sun. Reconnection releases huge amounts of energy resulting in high speeds plasma leaving the Sun.
The energy is increased as the magnetic eld topology becomes more and more twisted and complicated,
because it deviates from the relaxed state.
It is the frozen-in ux of plasma that follows the bent eld lines in the upper part of the solar atmosphere
e.g. between sunspots. It follows from Alfvén's frozen-in theorem, that states: "in a uid with innite
electric conductivity, magnetic eld lines are frozen into the uid and have to move along with it". So when
a eld line rises, the plasma follows the motion. Plasma can as mentioned be seen as a uid, which makes
it possible to see the behaviour of the eld lines in x-ray images. It is observed between bipolar sunspot
groups where the powerful magnetic elds in the sunspots are connected by ux tubes. The moment of
reconnection can sometimes be observed as a bright ash. These ashes are called ares.
An other important structure in space is the force-free ux rope. Is is closely related to the ux tube,
but it is more complicated. It is magnetic eld lines, which are twisted around an axis giving it a helical
structure. The ux ropes are created perpendicular to the uplifted magnetic eld lines in the upper part of
the solar atmosphere. There are two theories of how a ux rope is created: "The photospheric boundary
model" and "The open boundary model". The process of both can best be described best in a few steps
as seen in Figure 2.1.
The photospheric boundary model
At rst a group of magnetic eld lines rises from the surface from e.g. a bipolar sunspot pair. Over time
they rise high above the surface and the eld lines become close to parallel near the solar photospheric
boundary. At a point the magnetic eld lines, which are behaving as ux tubes, will reconnect. The
eld lines which are "captured" in the middle will be twisted into the helical structure of a ux rope and
still be connected to the solar magnetic eld. The reconnection is necessary to create a ux rope. The
conguration can exist for a long time giving the ux rope time to twist a lot. Further stretching of the
magnetic eld line surrounding the ux rope can lead to another reconnection, especially if the ux rope
moves upwards and increases the stretching. The structure is erupted away from the surface as a CME
due to the force from the reconnection, but the ux rope keeps its structure and sometimes also the anchor
in the solar surface [1].
The open boundary model
A ux tube is located in the deeper layers of the Sun. Due to magnetic buoyancy the ux rope is pushed
towards the surface and as the rst eld lines crosses it, they expand because of the lower magnetic eld
strength. The arcs are seen in the photosphere. The pressure from below continues, helped by convection
in the underlying layer, and a ux rope is formed when the center of the ux tube has emerged [1].
An illustration of these models can be seen in Figure 2.1 and 2.2. In 2D, the ux rope is always projected
as a closed eld line, but in reality it is a helical structure of the dierent components twisting within each
other. The yellow arc in Figure 2.2 shows how the 3D structure looks. The magnetic eld components will
be wrapped around the arc. The physical consequences of the magnetic eld mechanisms such as ares
and coronal mass ejections will be addressed later on.
The exact formation process of the ux rope structures is not of high relevance for this project, but the
magnetic signatures are. Therefore the formation will not be discussed further, but it is noted to be another
unsolved mystery in the space weather eld of research.
When the ux rope model is applied to solar eruptions, it is assumed to be force-free. It means that
J = αB, where α is a spatial constant describing the helicity, J is the current density and B is the
magnetic eld density. The ux rope remains force-free even under the inuence of a velocity on the
surface and J × B = 0 remains. [44]. Frozen-in ux and force free ux ropes are very important physical
principles applied in space weather research. Their application will be described in the following part.

2.2
Space weather
Space weather is a phenomena which can always be discussed, since the solar wind is always present. The
challenge is the great variety of behaviours of the relevant contributors. The term "space weather" is
relatively new compared to the history of terrestrial-heliospheric research. A Japanese Scientist Tsugunobi
Nagai used the term when he published the article "Space Weather Forecast: Prediction of Relativistic
Electron Intensity at Synchronous Orbit" in 1988. The name was quickly accepted around the world be-
cause the name was more appealing to the general public than "solar-terrestrial physics" [45].
The characteristics of the space weather phenomena will be presented here to give an understanding of
concept, but rst the solar cycle and its importance will be presented.
2.3
The solar cycle
The Sun releases ions, electrons and radiation at all time. The outer layers of the Sun are very dynamic
and from here the "wind" is emanating. The nature of the solar wind varies greatly over time - both on
the long and short term.
The solar cycle of about 11 years is a well-known and semi-predictable variation. The cycles are almost
equal in length and include a period of ascending solar activity, peak activity and descending solar activity
between two minima. Right now the 24th solar cycle is close to its nal minimum as seen Figure 2.3. The
cycle numbering started in February 1755 based on the numbering proposed by Rudolf Wolf in the middle
of the 19th century. The rst 100 years are a bit uncertain because the data is back-traced and based on
dierent sunspot observations. R. Wolf formulated the relative sunspot number, which is still used today.
It is calculated each day based on solar observations and the monthly average makes it possible to see the
variations over time. The sunspots are the dening feature for the solar cycle. In peak times there are
many sunspots indicating an "active" Sun. During minimum there can be periods with no sunspots, which
shows a "quiet" state of the Sun. It can be seen in Figure 2.3, where the percentage of the Sun covered by
sunspots is used as the observed parameter. The approximately 11 year cycle is obvious, where the overall
variation in the strength of the solar cycles can be seen too. R. Wolf was inspired by Heinrich Schwabe
who proposed this 11 year variation in 1844. [46]. Sunspots have been counted since 1610 [46].
Figure 2.3: The solar cycles displayed by the percentage of the solar surface covered by sunspots [3].
The sunspots are not directly linked to space weather phenomena, but they are important indicators of
the activity level of the Sun. The expected disturbances in the solar wind varies with the solar cycle too.
Therefore both detection and warning systems are recommend to consider this if they are to be precise at
all times.
The magnetic eld of the Sun is also changing during the solar cycle, which was proved by the Ulysses mis-
sion (read more: http://sci.esa.int/ulysses/). This is important for the particle motion as described
in section 2.1. The magnetic eld of the Sun is approximately a tilted dipole eld with a current sheet in
the region where the opposite polarities meet. During the quiet period the dipole is nicely ordered and the
magnetic poles are close to the geographic poles. Later on when the activity increases, so does the chaotic
==PAGE:25==
2.3 The solar cycle
9
behaviour in the magnetic eld structure. The magnetic poles will wander and the source of fast solar
wind will change partly to eruptions. A well known fact is that the polarity of the dipole eld changes
every solar cycle giving a magnetic cycle of 22 years. Ulysses showed that it takes months to turn the eld
around. This happens during minimum. The observations made by Ulysses can be seen in Figure 2.4. The
gure also shows the cross section of the velocity distribution around the Sun. In the quiet period where
coronal holes dominate, the fast stream come from higher latitudes. It is also here the open eld lines are
present, which allows the plasma to escape. In active periods the velocity distribution is more even, but
unstructured, which is directly corresponding to the distribution of active areas during the maximum. The
polarity of the magnetic eld is also shown in the two scenarios where minimum have a dipole structure.
At maximum the polarity of the solar magnetic eld changes over the whole cross section because of the
chaotic behaviour of the magnetic eld with active areas such as sunspots and wandering poles.
Figure 2.4: Observations of the solar magnetic eld made by Ulysses during two orbits, one during a quiet
period and one during maximum. The corresponding monthly average sunspot number is shown below [4].
During quiet period, where the surface is relatively calm over larger time periods, coronal holes can spread
from the poles to lower latitudes. Coronal holes are areas in the corona where large amounts of plasma are
released out in the heliosphere, which then appear darker on the surface of the Sun, since the temperature
here is lower due to "missing" plasma. The magnetic eld lines are open, which gives free passage for the
plasma. An example of this can be seen in Figure 2.5, where the Solar Dynamic Observatory, SDO (read
more: https://sdo.gsfc.nasa.gov/), has captured a coronal hole in the AIA (Atmospheric Imaging
Assembly) X-ray spectrum at 193 Angstroms. Coronal holes are important because they are the source of
high speed streams entering the heliosphere. The stability of a coronal hole can be used to determine the
origin of the high speed stream because the main polarity in the plasma will correspond to the contributing
hemisphere. For comparison a image of the Sun from the active period is seen to the right in Figure 2.5,
where there are no coronal holes, but many active regions shining bright due to reconnection processes.
The solar cycle is important for space weather because the activity of the Sun correlates with space weather
phenomena. The two primary sources of extreme space weather conditions are Stream Interaction Regions
==PAGE:26==
10
Theoretical background
Figure 2.5: Left: Image taken by SDO on the 29th of June 2016 showing a coronal hole extending towards
equator in a narrow passage. Right: Image from SDO showing the active Sun on the 24th of September
2011. The images are from iSWA [5], administrated by NASA.
(SIRs) and Interplanetary Coronal Mass Ejections (ICMEs). Both phenomena are seen during the entire
solar cycle, but SIRs dominate during the quiet period because they are created by high speed streams
from coronal holes interacting with the ambient solar wind. This will be elaborated in section 2.6. The
stability of a coronal hole is also important for the repeating of a SIR. If the same coronal hole exist for one
or more solar rotations, the SIR will be called a Co-rotating Interaction Region, CIR. Near the maximum,
ICMEs are often observed in all ranges of intensity. The very powerful ICMEs are seen near the peak
activity, which means that the risk of severe impacts are higher here. The reason for this will be explored
in section 2.5
2.4
The solar wind
As mentioned earlier, the solar wind consists of plasma, mostly ionised hydrogen. The dierence in gas
pressure between the interplanetary space and the corona drives the plasma outwards. This is despite the
gravity of the Sun [47]. The solar wind can be characterised in terms of average properties. These are
important for recognising special transients because they will dier a lot from some or all of the properties.
Solar wind exists in the whole heliosphere, but naturally varies with radial distance as the plasma expands.
In relation to space weather at Earth, the properties at 1 AU = 149.6 × 106 km are most relevant and these
are display in Table 2.4. In many cases the collected temperature and density will be used, because MHD
consider the plasma as one quasi-neutral uid.
Table 2.1: Solar wind properties at 1 AU.
Parameter

1.4 × 105 K
Electron density
7.1 cm−3
Proton temperature
1.2 × 105 K
Proton density
6.6 cm−3
In the event-less solar wind, the magnetic eld lines are close to a dipole in the lower latitudes. Near
the poles the eld lines are open with opposite polarity at each hemisphere. When looking at the Sun in
==PAGE:27==
2.4 The solar wind
11
coronagraphs, an instrument used to only observed the corona, the equatorial regions glow brightly due to
the plasma streaming out. They are called helmet streamers because they look like a pointy helmet on each
side of the Sun. They have the conical shape because the plasma here follows the closed eld lines, but due
to the dierence in pressure in the corona and the interplanetary space, some still continues to stream out,
forming the pointy end to the "hat". The helmet streamers are obscured during peak activity time since
many new sources of out-streaming plasma occur. The state of the Sun can be observed in LASCO (Large
Angle and Spectrometric COronagraph) onboard SOHO (SOlar and Heliospheric Observatory, read more
http://sci.esa.int/soho/ and https://www.swpc.noaa.gov/products/lasco-coronagraph). Exam-
ples of the quiet and active Sun observed by LASCO can be seen in Figure 2.6, where the dierence is
obvious. The helmet streamers are inclined because the magnetic axis is tilted compared to the geograph-
ical axis. Otherwise not much activity is seen. The opposite is true for the example of the active Sun.
Several out-streaming regions can be seen and a CME has just erupted. The balloon shaped structure, a
CME, to the upper left side arises because the ux rope is pushed outwards by a high speed, but it is still
anchored in the Sun as sketch in Figure 2.2. CMEs can almost always be observed in LASCO and it gives
the rst sign that an event might be coming. By using similar images from STEREO A and B (read more:
https://stereo.gsfc.nasa.gov/), a better understanding of the CME can be obtained.
Figure 2.6: Left: LASCO image of the quiet Sun, 21/08/2018, where the helmet streamers are visible on
each side of the Sun. Right: LASCO image of the active Sun, 20/08/2012, where several streamer are
present and a CME has just erupted as balloon shaped structure [5].
The ow of the solar wind is inuenced by the rotation of the Sun. As the solar wind ows outwards,
it expands, but the ux is still conserved as the frozen-in ux principle prescribes. This means that the
topology is conserved under the motion caused by rotation of the Sun. The source position of a stream is
xed and as a result the eld lines are dragged into a spiral form. These are called Parker spirals after their
discovery in the 1950s by E. N. Parker [47]. They can be described as spirals of Archimedes by considering
the situation from the perspective of the rotating Sun. The plasma is still moving radially outwards with
velocity vr = v(r). Since the frame of reference has been moved, the plasma also have a rotational velocity
in the ecliptic plane vφ = −ωr. ω is the angular rotation speed of the Sun which is ω = 2.87 × 10−6 s−1.
It is equal to the rotational speed of the solar surface. r is the relevant radial distance e.g. 1 AU.
Since the ux is frozen in, it can be related to the magnetic eld also.

= −ωr

= tan(θ)
(2.1)
θ is the Parker spiral angle as seen in Figure 2.7. It is the angle between strictly radial movement and
the backwards bending of the stream [6]. High speeds results in small Parker spiral angles as the plasma
moves far out in the heliosphere fast. It can be used to make a crude estimate of the velocity based on
==PAGE:28==
12
Theoretical background
the magnetic eld components. The measurement of the Parker spiral angle along with a comparison of
the shape of the Parker spirals at 400 kms−1 and 2000 kms−1 can be seen in Figure 2.7. The Parker spiral
angle is clearly smaller for large velocities.
Figure 2.7: Left: The denition of the Parker spiral angle, here θ = 45°. Right: A simulation of the Parker
spirals at two solar wind speeds. The rst circle the Earth's orbit, the next is Venus' [6].
The Parker spiral angle is dened from the solar point of view. To use it in space weather predictions on
Earth, the component must be translated into a terrestrial point of view. The interplanetary magnetic
eld is measured in the GSE (Geocentric Solar Ecliptic) coordinate system or the GSM (Geocentric Solar
magnetospheric) coordinate system [48]. They are very similar. The x-axis for both is the straight line
from the center of the Earth towards the Sun. The GSE z-axis point towards the north ecliptic pole, which
means yearly variation due to the tilted rotation axis of the Earth. The y-axis completes the right-hand
system, so that the x-y plane is in the ecliptic plane. The y-axis for the GSM system is perpendicular
to the Sun-Earth-line. The z-axis points towards the magnetic south pole (towards geographic north). It
means that it precesses over time [19]. A sketch can be seen in Appendix D. When only the ecliptic plane
is considered, the two y-axes are conveniently projected onto each other. It simplies the conversion to a
change of sign on By. For the the angle to be in the right quadrant, Bx and By must have opposite sign.
tan(θ) = −By
Bx
(2.2)
The Parker spirals are important for the propagation of material away from the Sun, also the high speed
streams that sometimes occur. When the streams interact with the ambient solar wind, interesting things
will happen. Other sudden eruptions of large plasma clouds might disturb the picture because of their
large speeds and magnetic eld congurations. These will be described in the following sections.
2.5
Interplanetary coronal mass ejections
The most extreme, powerful and fascinating events taking place on the Sun are the coronal mass ejections.
Here plasma is ejected at velocity up to thousands of kilometres per second carrying strong magnetic elds.
They can be seen as bright white balloon-shaped eruptions in the coronagraphs [49]. An example is also
visible in Figure 2.6. They are often seen after a are and was therefore mistaken to be a product of ares.
Since the rst indirect discovery in 1859 by R. Carrington and R. Hodgson, who linked the are observed
at the Sun to the geomagnetic storm following approximately 18 hours later, to the rst coronagraph ob-
servation in 1971 to now, many new things have been discovered. The primary discoveries are that CMEs
can happen without a are appearing rst and ares do not cause geomagnetic storms, other phenomena
like ICMEs do [17]. The invention of coronagraphs has accelerated the knowledge of the outer parts of the
Sun, because it makes it possible to see the corona and events like CMEs. Interplanetary CME (ICME) is
the term used for CMEs after they leave the Sun and propagate out in the heliosphere [49]. Triangulation
==PAGE:29==
2.5 Interplanetary coronal mass ejections
13
can be used to analyse the trajectory if the CME is observed by minimum two spacecraft [50]. It can
give an estimation of the velocity and direction, but it cannot stand alone. NASA has developed one
of such tools, based on data from LASCO and STEREO A and B, which can be accessed for free here:
https://ccmc.gsfc.nasa.gov/analysis/stereo/. Dierent propagation models can also be used, but
before additional observation and identication in the solar wind data nothing is known for certain. The
exact nature of the plasma is also unknown. This includes the magnetic eld conguration, which is very
important for space weather forecasting. Therefore the spacecraft positioned in Lagrange point 1, L1 seen
in Figure 3.3, are very important for the prediction of impacts. Forecasting is also an area where intense
research is going on today. This project will augment existing methods.
The "ideal ICME" consists of three main parts: Shock front, sheath region and ejecta. These can be seen
in the conceptual drawing in Figure 2.8. The boundary of the ICME becomes a shock front if the velocity
dierence between the ICME and the ambient solar wind is high enough. This is not always the case. The
shock can be seen in solar wind data as an abrupt simultaneous jump in the magnetic eld, velocity, or
density [38]. Since the shock only appears if the velocity is high enough, they are important precursors for
strong and powerful events. It has been concluded in several studies that there is a correlation between
shocks and potential dangerous impact [7, 37, 34]. As the sketch in Figure 2.8 also indicates, the shock can
extend further out than the ejecta in the middle. Therefore a shock is often, but not always and indicator
for a transient coming [49]. Shocks can also be on the front of SIRs, which will be described later.
Figure 2.8: Conceptual drawing of an ICME (MC) with the shock front, sheath region and ux rope
structured ejecta [7].
The sheath region is a very chaotic part of the ICME. High energy plasma is caught between the ejecta
and the shock. The magnetic eld, velocity and density measurements stay elevated here. The plasma is
compressed, which can give a very chaotic behaviour in the magnetic eld. The main part of the ICME is
the ejecta, which is a cloud of plasma caught in a magnetic eld conguration. The conguration shown
on Figure 2.8 refers to a special kind of ICMEs called Magnetic Clouds (MCs). They are very important
because then have been noted to cause severe impacts on Earth [51, 36]. They were identied as a subgroup
of ICMEs in 1981 by L. Burlaga [35]. Three necessary criteria for identication [52] were dened:.
1. The magnetic eld rotates smoothly during an interval in the order of 24 hours.
2. Magnetic eld increases above average.
3. The temperature is lower than average.
==PAGE:30==
14
Theoretical background
The smooth rotating eld occurs because the plasma is caught in a ux rope with helical magnetic eld
lines. This is a very central part of the MC. Models show that the magnetic behaviour can be represented
to a good accuracy as a force-free ux rope. These are described in section 2.1. The direction of the Bz
component of the magnetic eld is extremely important for the possible impact on Earth. This will later
be described in detail. Therefore, the rotation pattern of MCs have been divided into two groups with a
total of 8 dierent subgroups. If the storm is unipolar, it means it will keep a constant orientation of Bz.
If it is bipolar, then the Bz component will rotate 180° during the observation. The groups can be seen in
table 2.2, where the letters are abbreviations of North, South, East, and West [36]. The rotation is taking
place in the y-z plane.
Table 2.2: Conguration possibilities for magnetic clouds.
Unipolar
Bipolar
ENW
NES
WNE
NWS
ESW
SEN
WSE
SWN
It is not possible to determine, which type of conguration is present before the MC is observed from L1.
Since the southwards direction is the geoeective direction, then it might not be coming before the middle
of the event, if it is bipolar. MCs do generally have a higher magnitude of the magnetic eld. That also
increases their potential danger. The average of the magnetic eld in a MC is 12.6 nT compared to 8.9 nT
in ICMEs which are not categorised as MCs [7]. Compared to the average of the magnetic eld in the
solar wind from table 2.4, it is still a considerable increase. The distribution of MC clouds in the total
number of ICMEs is not even at all times. The investigation of the 23rd solar cycle, as I. Richardson
and H. Cane carried out, showed that the MC fraction is anti-correlated to the solar cycle. The fraction
increases around minimum where the total number of ICMEs decreases [7]. This is interesting since the
most powerful ICMEs and also MCs are registered near the maximum of the cycle. This is because the
ux rope structure also can exist for less extreme cases. These may still cause an impact because the Bz
component can be southwards.
ICMEs are in general not known for following the Parker spiral motion. Often the speed is too high and
it propagates radially outwards. Due to the frozen-in ux and conserved topology, the magnetic eld
structure of the ICME and the ambient solar wind do not merge. The transit time depends on the speed,
which means the fastest CMEs can hit Earth after a little more than half a day. This is one of the main
challenges because the fastest ICMEs usually are more powerful and likely very dangerous. Most ICMEs
take in the order of days to arrive. From L1, where most space weather satellites are located it only gives
between a half an hour to a couple of hours to react [34]. Then it takes a little more time before the
geomagnetic storm is at its maximum. ICMEs are very important within the framework of space weather.
They dominate the forecasts during solar maximum and some of the most extreme impacts have been
caused by these, but stream interaction regions are also known to contribute signicantly to disturbances,
especially during solar minimum. Therefore the characteristics of those will be presented in the following
section.
2.6
Stream interaction regions
Stream Interactions Regions (SIRs) occur when a coronal hole expands and a high speed stream is streaming
out near the equatorial plane. It enters the ambient solar wind at a much higher speed, which means it
quickly catches up with the slow solar wind ahead and builds up plasma in the front of itself. The structure
gives rise to a SIR, which is characterised by a rapid rise in the total perpendicular pressure, the sum of
the magnetic and thermal pressure, of the solar wind. The density also increases in the compressed region
until the Stream Interface (SI), which is the center of the SIR. A conceptual drawing of a SIR can be seen
in Figure 2.9. At the SI, the peak of the pressure can be seen. The velocity increases over the course of the
entire SIR because the high speed stream is still coming from upstream [8]. Therefore several parameters
are combined to detect SIRs. The velocity will continue to be elevated after the SIR has passed. There is a
space behind the SIR consisting of low density plasma with high velocities. This part is called rarefaction.
==PAGE:31==
2.6 Stream interaction regions
15
Figure 2.9: Conceptual drawing of a SIR, highlighted in grey, where the magenta line is the SI. The length
of the arrow indicates the speed [8].
The B eld will often peak at the same time as the SI . The magnetic eld is compressed because the
magnetic eld lines in the SIR and the solar wind do not mix as described by the conserved topology in
MHD (Section 2.1). The compression means that the dierent components of the magnetic eld will show
very chaotic behaviour. The many uctuations continue after the SI and often intensies. See example
in appendix F. The SIR is separated from the ambient solar wind because of their dierent origin on the
Sun. It results in dierent magnetic conguration, which gives a boundary between the two parts. As the
conceptual sketch in Figure 2.9 shows, the high speed stream will also follow the Parker spiral motion.
This is important if the coronal hole displays stability and exists for more than one solar rotation. The SIR
will then reappear after 25-29 days. After that it will be called a Co-rotating Interaction Region (CIR) to
indicate that is it a stable structure. Some articles only talk about CIRs, but it is misleading since CIRs
are only about 50% of the SIRs observed [8, 18]. The fraction of CIRs compared to SIRs changes over the
years. The occurrence of SIR and CIRs are also related to the solar cycle. In the investigation carried out
by L. Jian of the 23rd solar cycle some trends can be seen [8]. The minimum number of observations is
seen around solar maximum. The highest number of observations is seen in the declining phase.
SIRs are also known to create shocks. Once again it happens when the speed of the plasma in the high
speed stream is much larger than the solar wind speed. The features of the shocks are similar to those
of the ICME. One major dierence is that shock can occur both in front of the SIR called a forward
shock, and on the back side, which is then called reverse shock. The forward shock is the most frequent to
observe. Some events have both, and few only have a reverse shock. The reverse shock appears because
plasma is streaming towards the rarefaction after the SI, where the velocity is still high. That can form a
shock going upstream [8]. The number of fast forward shocks are clearly following the solar cycle, but it is
because the shocks from ICMEs are part of this statistic and they peak at maximum activity. The number
of fast forward shocks driven by SIRs varies less, but they show an increase a couple of years after the
maximum and near minimum [34]. The dierent shock parameters do not show specic tendencies over
time. The shocks generated by ICMEs are more powerful, than those driven by SIR, but both are still
important for space weather purposes. Observations, made by spacecraft further out in the solar system,
have shown that many SIRs rst develop shocks when the ambient solar wind has decreased in speed [53].
This is mostly relevant for missions taking place in these areas. Even though SIRs can be stable and turn
into CIRs it is not guaranteed that the shock appear every time or even between dierent spacecraft in
the same rotation. By comparing data from STEREO A and B in the beginning of the mission, it was
==PAGE:32==
16
Theoretical background
concluded that the local nature of the solar wind is important [54]. Even though changes might occur,
the principle of several spacecraft looking for SIRs/CIRs is interesting. Since they follow the Parker spiral
motion, it will give extra forecasting time. A good place to do this would be Lagrange point 5 because it is
upstream of Earth in terms of charged particle streams from the Sun and it is stable like L1. The European
Space Agency (ESA) has approved funding for to designing such a mission. The decision to send it to L5
is not nal yet, but it oers some great opportunities for improving forecasts [55]. It could also improve
the understanding of observed CMEs by triangulation, if the spacecraft includes a coronagraph, since the
Sun would be continuously observed from a dierent angle. Ideally another space weather observation
spacecraft would be positioned in L4 to give a view of both sides of the Sun.
2.7
Geoeectiveness and geomagnetic storms
ICMEs and SIRs are spectacular phenomena and they are an important part of heliospheric research.
Scientists all over the world work hard to better understand the creation and development of these.
This becomes even more relevant when space weather is considered. Since geomagnetic activity in the
magnetosphere of the Earth was discovered in 1859 to be linked to the activities of the Sun, both elds
have been closely related [49].
The magnetic eld of the Earth can be modelled as a dipole eld for many practical purposes. It gives
a shell of protection from the constant solar wind and radiation from space, but it is not completely
impervious. Geomagnetic activity arises when charged particles from the Sun enter the upper layers of the
magnetosphere and create disturbances. When the plasma of the solar wind arrives with large speed and
strong magnetic eld it can cause severe problems on Earth. Therefore predicting the geoeectiveness of
solar event is crucial. The geoeectiveness is a term used to describe the potential risk of a specic type
of events to create disturbances in the magnetosphere and create a geomagnetic storm [56]. Normally it
is calculated as the number of events causing a storm compared to the total number of events [57]. In
some cases geoeectiveness implies how the "input" from the disturbance will correspond to the "output".
This is better described as the eciency of the disturbance to create a geomagnetic storm [57]. It will be
called geoeciency in the following when specic impacts of event are considered. A spectacular sight is
the aurora lights caused by geomagnetic storms in the polar area as the picture seen in Figure 2.10 is an
example of. The stunning colors are results of atoms exited by solar electrons in the atmosphere and then
decaying while emitting light.
Figure 2.10: Aurora observed in the U.S. in August 2011 with both green and red colours [9].
==PAGE:33==
2.7 Geoeectiveness and geomagnetic storms
17
Before analysing geoeectiveness and geomagnetic impacts in depth it is necessary to understand the
conguration of the magnetosphere. In Figure 2.11 the many component of the magnetosphere can be
seen.
The magnetosphere is crucial for life on Earth while also being home to some very interesting
phenomena such as radiation belts and aurora.
Figure 2.11: The magnetosphere of the Earth from the bow shock to the ionosphere, and the magnetotail
extending away from the Sun [10].
The complex structure starts at the bow shock located between the Earth and the Sun. Here the supersonic
particles from the solar wind are deected by the magnetic pressure from the magnetic eld. This region
located between 9 - 14 Re (Earth radius = Re = 6371 km) out. The variation occurs because the pressure
from the solar wind changes a lot over time. The magnetosphere is the outermost part of the magnetic
eld. The position of the magnetosphere changes from 5.2 Re to 14 Re. If a powerful event arrives from
the Sun, it can compress the magnetospause below the 6.6 Re of the geosynchronous orbit and expose the
satellites placed there. It is also in the magnetosphere that the two van Allen belts are located. In Figure
2.11 the radiation belts as they are called too, are located in the intense magenta region around Earth.
They were the primary discovery by the U.S.'s rst satellite Explorer 1 in 1958 [11]. The outer belt is
mainly free electron whereas the inner belt is mostly protons. The structure of the van Allen belts can be
seen in Figure 2.12. The inner belt is approx. 1.5 Re out and the outer belt is centred around 3.5 Re away.
The inner belt consists of protons from atmospheric particles interacting with cosmic rays. Many of the
particles in the outer belt are originally from the solar wind, but have been captured by the mirror eect
[11]. The mirror eect occurs because the trapped particles gyrates along the eld lines. The intensity of
the magnetic eld increases close to the poles, where the eld lines converge, and it reduces the parallel
energy of the particles, until it is all transformed to perpendicular energy. Then the particle is mirrored
and starts to move backwards, increasing the perpendicular energy again. The particles in the magneto-
sphere moves back and forth several times every second, until friction causes them to loose enough energy
to enter in the magnetosphere. Particles with high energy can escape the mirror eect easier because the
eld intensity at the poles is not enough to stop them. The mirror eect is illustrated in Figure 2.13. The
==PAGE:34==
18
Theoretical background
Figure 2.12: Cross section of the two Van Allen belts (inner and outer) around Earth consisting of protons
and electrons respectively [11].
drift of charged particles creates a current, which goes around the Earth in the equatorial plane called
the ring current. As seen in Figure 2.13, the protons and electrons moves in opposite direction in the
ring current. The ring current changes when a geomagnetic storm is happening. This is used as a way of
measuring the strength of the geomagnetic activity.
Figure 2.13: A conceptual sketch of the trapped particles experiencing the mirror eect near the poles [12].
The next layer is the plasmasphere. This is a region of cold plasma. The ionosphere is the inner part of
the magnetosphere. It is always ionised, mainly due to UV light from the Sun. If a powerful event hits
Earth, charged particles can enter the ionosphere and change the distribution of ions and electrons. The
==PAGE:35==
2.7 Geoeectiveness and geomagnetic storms
19
particles will enter at the poles where the eld lines are open. This is called the cusp and can be seen in
Figure 2.11, where it appears both on the north and south pole. For the solar wind to even approach the
cusp and the lower magnetosphere, the Bz component of the IMF has to be negative in order to reconnect
with the magnetic eld of the Earth. If the IMF is positive, it will be like forcing two north poles together
from two bar magnets. If the reconnection happen, it gives rise to some interesting current systems in the
magnetosphere that will not be described here. It also creates the auroral oval, which is an semi-symmetric
band of charged particles around the magnetic north and south pole respectively, where the atoms are ex-
cited and creates aurora. The extent varies a lot with the solar activities. If a geomagnetic storm is taking
place, the auroral oval can extend far towards equator. Aurora is the visible result of geomagnetic storms.
A beautiful example can be seen in Figure 2.11, where the oval extend down to the southern states of the
U.S. The magnetosphere of the Earth is not symmetric, as known from a dipole. The solar wind drags the
magnetic eld away from the Sun when it deects on the bow shock. It is called the magnetotail and is,
like the magnetosphere, very dynamic over time.
A geomagnetic storm does not only create beautiful aurora as seen in Figure 2.10, it can also have fatal
impact on the infrastructure. As mentioned, high velocity events can compress the magnetopause and
expose satellite to charged particles and strong magnetic elds. It may damage the electronics and in the
worst case make the satellite useless afterwards. A warning before the storm hits gives the operators time
to either turn the satellite to shield the most sensitive parts or shut it down. That can prevent the most
severe damage.
When the magnetosphere is disturbed, electromagnetic signals will propagate dierently. That is very
important for the Global Navigation Satellite Systems (GNSS) such as the European "Galileo" and the
American "GPS". Since the technology is based on measuring propagation delays, then a change in path
length due lower propagation speed of radio signals when passing through a dispersive medium will give a
wrong position. As GNSS becomes more and more integrated in society, the more damage a geomagnetic
storm can cause. Especially if the technologies are not design to meet the threat. There has already been
recorded some problem with radar tracking of aeroplanes in Greenland and Sweden [33]. At the incident
in 2015, the Danish airports did not experience any problems, which shows the disturbances can be very
local. A break in the schedule an international airport is a very costly and complex aair. Therefore new
systems based on GPS technology, but with safety systems, are being tested called GBAS (Ground Based
Augmentation System). It takes space weather impacts into account. GBAS has been proven useful in the
U.S. and is now being implement in several places over the world [58].
Another threat is Geomagnetically Induced Currents (GIC). There are a few ferromagnetic materials in
the periodic system (among other iron, cobolt, nickel), which can be found in the upper layers of the
Earth's crust. It can be found in pure form or mostly in chemical compounds. If the ionosphere above the
ground is experiencing strong disturbances, it will induce currents in the ground, that in the end can make
large currents run in the electric grid if cables are placed over or in the ground. An extra high current
can burn down the transformers that control the high voltage systems in the long distance power lines
or other equipment. If it happens for a large area it can cause serious black-outs. The longer the high
voltage cables are, the large the currents can build up in the power grid. This was investigated by G. S.
Kelly et al. for the high-voltage transmission system in UK and France [31]. They concluded that cutting
of connections between transformer stations can minimise the induced current. There is also a dierence
between isolated system and well-connected system, where the latter is more complicated to control. An
example is the European mainland. The models show maximum current of several hundreds of Amperes,
which can be severe for transformer stations. GIC can also accelerate the corrosion rate of pipelines, which
is not fatal, but it can lead to leakage, which is expensive to locate and repair. The GICs must also be
considered when constructing railways since the tracks can experience induced currents too. This is not
known as a major problem [59].
When geomagnetic activity was rst noticed by Carrington in 1859 and associated with the Sun, the
telegraph lines broke down and compasses was noted to act strange. They had troubles aligning to the
Earth magnetic eld because the atmosphere was lled with random charged particles [60]. This is one
of the largest known geomagnetic storms. If that happened today, the damage could be worth more than
2 trillion dollars and completely paralyse the society for weeks. This includes damage of the power grid,
disturbance in GNSS signals, damaged or destroyed satellites [17], no air trac possible, damaged pipe
lines by GIC, and more. Therefore space weather forecasting is very important, and society is slowly
adapting to resisting the "perfect storm" like the one 150 years ago [60]. Scientists hope to be able to
understand the Sun to an extent, which makes it possible to predict when it will hit next. There is no
doubt that another "Carrington event" will come, the question is when.
==PAGE:36==
20
Theoretical background
To categorise geomagnetic activity several indices have been introduced. The smart reason for several
indices is that they describe the geomagnetic activity based on dierent parameters and at dierent lati-
tudes. The two most popular indices are Disturbance storm index (Dst index) and Kp. The Dst index is a
weighted average of the deviation from the quiet level of the horizontal magnetic eld given every hour. It
is given in nT (nano Tesla). Four stations are recording data from low latitudes. The average is conducted
by World Data Center, (WDC), for Geomagnetism in Kyoto. There is also an equal index which is given
every minute called SYM-H. The Dst index is inuenced by the currents in the magnetosphere, where the
westward ring current driven by protons is the most dominant. During a geomagnetic storm, the ring
current is enhanced, which results in a negative deviation. Therefore the index is based on how negative
Dst gets [17].
The classication can be seen in table 2.3. The threshold values are those which Dst must decrease below.
The classication can vary a bit between studies, mostly in the classication name and number of classes
[20, 17, 56].
Table 2.3: Dst classications [20].
Threshold
Classication
−30 nT
Weak
−50 nT
Moderate
−100 nT
Strong
−200 nT
Severe
−350 nT
Great
Planetary K-index, Kp, is another popular index, which is based on the perturbation in K. K is an index
that has been dened to be related to magnetic observations. A three-hour interval is then calculated based
on 13 stations at mid-latitudes. It is useful because it is sensitive to the high-latitude auroral currents
that are aected by geomagnetic storms [17]. The classes of the Kp index is given in table 2.4. NOAA has
dened a G scale for storm sizes that is directly related to the Kp index.
Table 2.4: Kp classications [21].
Threshold
Classication
G class

Minor
G1
Kp = 6

Kp = 9
Extreme
G5
There is no direct link between the Dst and Kp indeces. The two indices are known to react dierently
to geomagnetic storms. If a storm induces strong currents in the auroral oval, but it does not aect the
ring current a lot, Kp will increase, but Dst might stay a little below the normal. Investigations by H.
E. J. Koskinen and K. E. J Huttunen show that Kp reacts more to the shock and sheath disturbances,
where Dst are sensitive to the ejecta [17]. Especially magnetic clouds give a higher Dst index compared
to the equivalent Kp value for the given storm, categorised as "intense". It was the opposite way around
for sheath generated storms. Their investigation of geomagnetic storms between 1997-2003 showed that
a high Kp index can happen without a signicant increase in the Dst index. It is not seen the other
way around [17]. Therefore one index cannot stand alone. The geoeectiveness of the dierent parts of
an ICME can be seen in Figure 3 in the article (also seen in Appendix B), where the importance of the
sheath region is emphasized. The sheath region becomes geoeective when the IMF is compressed with
an existing negative Bz inside.
It is well known that the most important parameter for geomagnetic activity is the Bz direction of the
IMF [61]. It has to be negative in order to reconnect with the magnetic eld of the Earth and allow
plasma to enter the magnetosphere. It is still a major challenge for space weather, that the Bz component
is unpredictable from afar. Only when the events reaches L1, the satellites can measure the direction of
the B-components. The duration of the negative Bz passage is important for intensifying the event [56].
==PAGE:37==
2.7 Geoeectiveness and geomagnetic storms
21
Therefore duration is often used as a secondary parameter for determining the geoeectiveness [18, 17].
Many dierent studies have investigated the relation between geomagnetic activity and the minimum of
Bz [37, 7]. It turns out that a linear relation can be found. The exact coecients vary a little depending on
the used dataset. An overview can be seen in table 2.5. C. Wu and R. Lepping [37] also did the regression
for dierent MCs separated by velocity. For high velocities (v ≥750 kms−1) the correlation coecient was
0.93, but there was only 8 events in the category though.
