The geoeectiveness of the dierent types of events have been investigated. Magnetic clouds are the most
geoeective and they are the drivers behind the majority of severe storms [21]. I. Richardson and H. Cane,
2011 [21] concluded that all severe storms had a Bz ≤−23 nT. A lower value of Bz could also result in a
severe storm if it lasted for a long time. Magnetic clouds are known to have long lasting negative Bz if it is
unipolar. Examination of Wind data by C. Wu and R. P. Lepping has shown that ≈90% of MCs induce
geomagnetic storms and ≈39% of MCs generates intense storms [37]. The average value of ⟨Bzmin⟩is
-15.3 nT between 1995-2012 for MC. The average value of Dst in the same period is -102 nT, which is a
strong storm. In general MCs that are led by a shock are more geoeective.
The main drivers of storms change over the solar cycle as described earlier. E. K. J. Kilpua [18] has
shown this graphically in Figure 6 (included in appendix C) in the study "Geoeective Properties of Solar
Transients and Stream Interaction Regions". The gure both shows that there is more geomagnetic storms
at maximum, and ICMEs are the primary driver in this period. The category presented are the G scale
which can be compared the Kp scale, which is done in table 2.4. The "extreme" storms are always driven
by ICMEs, but the fraction of storms driven by SIRs and CIRs increases signicantly in the quiet periods.
Thus they cannot be ignored in forecasting. Between 1964 and 2003 one third of the registered CIRs were
followed by at least a moderate geomagnetic storm. The region right after the stream interface turned
out to be the most geoeective because it could include strong southward magnetic eld, large speeds and
strongly uctuating IMF. Some of the strongest geomagnetic storms were caused by ICMEs caught by a
SIR/CIR or two ICMEs merging. These hybrid event can give long-lasting, high speed events with very
negative Bz values.
==PAGE:38==
 
==PAGE:39==
3
|
Data description
To complete this project, data from relevant satellites is necessary to build the program and later test
it .
In the beginning stored data from the OMNIweb and STEREO A and B is used.
Later on the
program must work in real time with DSCOVR data provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric
Administration, NOAA.
This chapter aims at describing magnetometer technology, the relevant data and the space missions, which
provide it.
3.1
Magnetometer
Figure 3.1:
Tri-axial uxgate magnetometer
sketch where the three identical parts are shown.
The red coil is the driver and the blue-grey coil
is the sense [13].
The magnetometer used for all the missions relevant to
this project is based on the tri-axial uxgate technology.
This technology has been implemented in many space
missions due to the many benets such as light weight,
3D vector output, low power consumption, high preci-
sion, and large measuring range.
The tri-axial uxgate magnetometer consists of three
identical, but separate, components measuring each di-
rection of the magnetic eld [62]. The outline of each
component is simple.
A toroidal core made from a
magnetic material is wrapped with a wire loop creat-
ing a ring coil. A second wire coil is surrounding the
core and rst coil as seen for each of the magnetome-
ters in Figure 3.1.
The inner coil is called the drive,
since it is connected to an AC generator, that drives
the system. The second (outer) coil is called a sense,
because it senses the surplus of the induced eld in
the core and reacts based on that.
The core is usu-
ally made of a nickel-iron alloy (mu-metal). The Mu-
metal has a high permeability, which is desired since
the magnetic eld, which is measured, can be very
weak.
A sketch of the principle can be seen in Figure 3.1 with three torus shaped cores with symmetry axis
perpendicular to one another in order to measure Bx, By, and Bz.
To understand the principle behind a uxgate magnetometer, it is easiest to imagine the toroidal core,
seen in Figure 3.1, consisting of two joined half-cores. When a current is send through the drive coil, two
equal and opposite directed magnetic elds will be induced in the two core parts and they will saturate
equally fast as seen in Figure 3.2. They cancel each other out during the whole swing of the AC current.
The sense coil will not react since no surplus magnetic eld exists.
If an external eld is present, it will change the saturation time of both of the two halves. During the
saturation, where the AC increases, the part with the eld directed in the same direction as the external
==PAGE:40==
24
Data description
eld will be saturated before the other half. Meaning the green part in 3.2 will saturate before the blue
part. It will create a small period with a surplus eld which then induces a voltage in the sense coil.
When the halves de-saturate, there will again be a surplus because the part with the eld in the same
direction as the external eld will take longer to reach zero eld strength. There are two spikes in voltage
for every saturation/de-saturation, which mean the frequency of the output signal is twice that of the AC
current. The height and phase of the voltage peaks are directly related the the strength and inclination of
the external eld.
The behaviour of the uxgate magnetometer in an environment with an external magnetic eld will be as
shown in Figure 3.2. The two parts' induced magnetic elds can be seen as a blue and a green line. The
green line in the rst arc represents the part which as aligned with the external eld. For the next arcs the
induced elds are in the opposite direction in each half, since the current changes direction. The external
eld in unchanged. The surplus magnetic eld shown in orange, which the sense coil reacts to show the
double frequency. The measured voltage are showed as the magenta line. By combining three dierent
uxgate magnetometers, a three dimensional view of the external magnetic eld can be sampled.
Figure 3.2: Left: sketch of the two half-cores and their induced elds with the drive coil. Right: Plot
of the eld observed in the two halves (blue, green), the surplus magnetic eld (orange), and the voltage
(magenta) measured in the sense coil when an external eld is present.
3.2
OMNIweb
The OMNIweb interface is a NASA initiative, where the data collected by several satellites is merged into
a single database with a single data interface. The primary area of research is heliophysics which among
other is the heliospheric environment and how it interacts with the magnetosphere of the Earth. The data
can be accessed as high (1 min or 5 min) or low (1 hour, 1 day or 27 days) resolution data. The sampling
rate of the dierent spacecraft varies a bit, also for dierent parameters. The magnetic eld data is often
measured several times per second, which means that even the high resolution data (1 min) is an average.
It can all be accessed online at https://omniweb.gsfc.nasa.gov/hw.html.
To be able to collect all the data in one output, it is shifted to the Earth bow shock nose between Earth
and the Sun giving it the same reference point. It is also done to support studies of the magnetosphere.
The high resolution data is available from 1981 till today and low resolution data can be found from 1963
till today. Over the years, the accuracy have improved a lot, which is relevant to have in mind.
In this project, both high and low resolution data is included from a period from 1998 till 2014 for initial
investigation and testing. In this period mainly 3 spacecraft contribute to the OMNIweb output, which is
relevant for the magnetic eld investigation. It is IMP 8, WIND, and ACE, all will be described briey
below. Later, real-time data from DSCOVR (see section 3.3) is introduced.
==PAGE:41==
3.2 OMNIweb
25
3.2.1
IMP 8
Interplanetary Monitoring Platform 8 (read more: https://science.nasa.gov/missions/imp-8) was the
last satellite in the IMP mission, which was launched to study the environment and interaction between
the Earth magnetic eld and the interplanetary space. The contribution from the Sun was a central part
of the focus, but the deep space contribution was also included. The IMP missions was an important
contribution to the early study of the space weather environment by e.g. tracking the solar cycle.
The orbit was elliptical, which meant that it observed the solar wind for 7-8 days out of the 12.5 days
orbital period. The last days were spent in the magnetotail of the Earth [63]. The IMP 8 was launched
in October 1973 and continued to deliver data until June 2000, but during the mission the data collection
was limited to one of the original three ranges because of instrument problems in 1975.
The magnetic eld monitoring experiment was called IMP-J and consisted of a boom-mounted tri-axial
uxgate magnetometer. The sensors operated in 3 dierent ranges: ±12 nT, ±36 nT and ±108 nT with an
digitization accuracy of ±0.3 nT in the mid-range, that ended up as the one used for the majority of the
time [64].
3.2.2
WIND
WIND (read more: https://wind.nasa.gov/) is a global geospace science spacecraft launched in Novem-
ber 1994 to study the interplanetary space around the Earth. The spacecraft was launched into an orbit
which crossed the near-lunar space and the Earth magnetosphere rst, since the original goal was the
Lagrange point 1, where ACE shortly after arrived. Therefore the scientic mission was changed a bit. In
May 2004, it arrived in L1 and will stay there till the mission is terminated.
WIND has 2 boom-mounted tri-axial uxgate magnetometers, which gives redundancy and better mea-
surements of weak magnetic elds. It also makes it possible to register and remove systematic error due
to magnetic elds generated by the electronic hardware in the satellite. The two magnetometers called
M1 and M2 have a dynamic range of sensitivity of ±4 nT to ±65 536 nT, which is the size of the magnetic
eld of the Earth. The Magnetic Field Instruments, MFI, on WIND have 8 dierent dynamic ranges from
±4 nT to ±65 536 nT with resolution between ±0.001 nT to ±16.0 nT. The output is separated into three
dierent types:
1. Continues vector sampling every 92 s
2. Snapshot memory data at 44 vectors per second
3. Fast Fourier Transformation at 44 vectors per second.
There is a small 12-bit microprocessor on board that collects the data and ensures that it is transmitted to
Earth. WIND snapshot data is transmitted every 0.0226 s and continuous monitored data is transmitted
either every 0.092 s, 3 s, 1 min or 1 hour depending on the mode of the transmitter. The same is valid for
the FFT elements. Everything is transmitted in both GSE and GSM coordinates [65]. The ACE data is
then extrapolated to the nose of the bow shock to be a part of the OMNIweb.
3.2.3
ACE
The Advanced Composition Explorer (ACE, read more: https://science.nasa.gov/missions/ace) was
designed and build to continue the monitoring of the interplanetary space. It was launched in August 1997
into a halo orbit around the Lagrange point 1 between the Earth and the Sun. Here it is possible to
constantly measure the solar wind plasma properties and the interplanetary magnetic eld.
ACE have 2 boom-mounted magnetometers which are the same as the pair used for WIND. They were a
spare pair that was modied a bit before they were attached to the spacecraft. One of the changes is the
sampling rates for the outputs [66], which now are:
1. Continues data at 3, 4 or 6 vectors per second
==PAGE:42==
26
Data description
2. Snapshot memory data at 24 vectors per second
3. Fast Fourier Transformation (FFT) at 24 vectors per second applied synchronously on blocks of 512
samples.
ACE transmits 6 vectors/second where the output is a ratio between M1 and M2 as 3:3, 5:1 or 6:0. The
snapshots can be transmitted as fast as 30 vectors per second. There are 3 dierent settings for transmitting
rate. The FFT data is transmitted every 17 s as an accumulation of FFT elements [67].
The ACE data is extrapolated to the bow shock nose by using the position of the bow shock and the
ambient solar wind speed.
3.3
DSCOVR
Deep Space Climate Observatory made by NASA (DSCOVR, read more:
https://www.nesdis.noaa.gov/content/dscovr-deep-space-climate-observatory) is the new satel-
lite in the eld of space weather observations. It was launched into space in February 2015 and will replace
ACE and provide real-time high precision data about the solar wind and the deep space radiation. It
is also placed in L1 which can be seen in Figure 3.3. The point is 1 millions miles (equal to 1.5 million
metres) from Earth, which will give a warning time of 15 min to a couple hours.
Figure 3.3: Schematic representation of the Earth-Sun system with the 5 stable Lagrange points, where
DSCOVR can be seen in L1 [14].
The plasma-magnetometer instrument on DSCOVR is called PlasMag, which measures the plasma pa-
rameter and the magnetic eld. The magnetometers are once again tri-axial uxgate technology. The
vector measurements are made in 30 and 40 milliseconds with a sensitivity of 0.1 nT and a time reso-
lution of 0.02 second. The magnetometer is mounted 2/3 out on a boom to remove contributions from
magnetic elds induced in the electronic hardware. [68]. It is the sub-organisation SWPC (Space Weather
Prediction Center), which manages the plot and analyses. The real time data can be downloaded here:
http://services.swpc.noaa.gov/products/solar-wind/.
==PAGE:43==
3.4 STEREO
27
3.4
STEREO
The Solar Terrestrial Relation Observatory is a double spacecraft mission, which investigates the helio-
sphere around the Sun. The two spacecraft were launched 2006 into the Earth orbit where STEREO A
was moving ahead of the Earth downstream and STEREO B behind upstream. STEREO A is moving
around 20 degrees away from Earth every year and STEREO B is only moving 10 degrees away. The
mission should measure the solar wind from another perspective and also observe the surface of the Sun.
By having three chronographs (including SOHO/LASCO) to detects coronal mass ejections, it is possible
to better determine speed, direction and origin of these. For space weather purposes it is interesting to
know more about the evolution of dierent phenomena during a solar rotation. [69]
The instrument for plasma and magnetic eld measurements are called IMPACT which gives a vector out-
put of the interplanetary magnetic eld. It is also a tri-axial uxgate magnetometer mounted on a boom.
It is a ring core geometry with molybdenum-alloy. The dynamic range is also divided into 8 smaller ranges
with tting threshold values to maximize sensitivity. The maximum range can measure the magnetic eld
of the Earth [70]. In 2014 the contact with STEREO B was lost. In 2016 contact was shortly re-established
and data showed that the spacecraft was spinning too fast. It was not possible to correct the motion at
that point. Recovery attempts in autumn 2016 have failed and therefore data is not transmitted from
STEREO B any more. The current position of STEREO A and B can be seen in Figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4: Position of STEREO A and B on the 21st of
August 2018. The spacecraft have moved more the 180° so
that B is now ahead and A is behind Earth [15].
The positions of STEREO A and B can be
shown on the website https://stereo-ssc.
nascom.nasa.gov/cgi-bin/make_where_gif
since it was launched. NASA has published
a website where it is possible to do triangu-
lation on ICMEs detected in STEREO A, B
and LASCO. It can be accessed here: http:
//ccmc.gsfc.nasa.gov/analysis/stereo/
==PAGE:44==
 
==PAGE:45==
4
|
Methodology
The aim of this project is to build a program that can identify disturbances in the solar wind, categorise
them based on selected criteria and estimate the potential impact on Earth. The process has taken it
origin in the synthesis project "Development of Method and Software for Real-time Observation of Solar
Wind Disturbances" conducted in Spring 2017 by myself [71]. It showed that it is possible to distinguish
disturbances, but the method needed to be developed further. At rst data from previous years saved in
the OMNIweb portal is used, both minute and hourly averaged. The same type of data from STEREO
A and B is included later. After the validation of the OMNIweb and STEREO data, real time DSCOVR
data is implemented into the program. The 7 days span will be chosen for this application. Since the time
span of this project is limited, DSCOVR data from 2016-2017 will be used to make simulated-real time,
where 7 day time spans are opened in steps. The project is carried out using Matlab R2018a. Matlab
is chosen because it was used in the synthesis project. The program also aims at working together with
AWARE, Automated WARnings of Earth arrivals: (http://swe.ssa.esa.int/heliospheric-weather),
a disturbance detection program developed by Susanne Vennerstrøm for ESA, which is coded in Matlab
too. Lastly Matlab has some built-in functions, which are useful. The methodology will be separated in
two parts since the rst part work with old data and the next part uses real time data. All the results
from the tests and validations will be presented in chapter 5 together with relevant plots of the data.
4.1
OMNIweb and STEREO data
The rst part of the project uses OMNIweb data from the last decades to develop the detection program.
To verify the behaviour and classication of the program, dierent catalogues presented in the literature
are used. The classication in the applied catalogue is manual, which will give rise to some dierences,
but they still serve as a good comparison. The following four catalogues have been used.
 I. Richardson and H. Cane, ICME catalogue, 1996-2015, presented in "Near-Earth Interplanetary
Coronal Mass Ejections During Solar Cycle 23 (1996-2009): Catalog and Summery of Properties" [7]
 Called RC catalogue in the following
 L. Jian et. al., SIR catalogue, 1995-2009, presented in "Properties of Stream Interactions at one AU
during 1996-2004" [8]
 Called Jian catalogue in the following
 NASA, SIR and ICME catalogues for STEREO A and C. They are accessible here: ICME - http:
//www-ssc.igpp.ucla.edu/~jlan/STEREO/Level3/LanJian_STEREO_ICME_List.txt, SIR - ftp:
//stereodata.nascom.nasa.gov/pub/ins_data/impact/level3/STEREO_Level3_SIR.pdf
 Called STES and STEI catalogue for SIRs and ICMEs respectively in the following sections
 AWARE disturbance list with categories handed out by Susanne Vennerstrøm personally for this
project
 Called AWARE list in the following
==PAGE:46==
30
Methodology
The rst step in the process was to open the data and nd the potential disturbances in the solar wind.
The data is obtained from yearly les for both minute and hourly averages. The desired time interval
for investigation is chosen in the beginning. They are transformed into Julian day time, (jd), to give
a continuous time scale by the function:
jd2000_new("year","month","day","hour"), provided by
Susanne Vennerstrøm. It is from AWARE and has its zero time at the 1/1 2000 00:00. Then the relevant
les are opened and the exact period of interest is extracted and collected in separate parameters for both
minute and hourly averages. This is important for the investigation, since the period of interest varies
between short and long. The extracted parameters are:
 Julian day time
 Magnitude of B
 Components of B: Bx, By and Bz
 SYM-H (Dst) data for minute (hourly) average
A potential disturbance is dened by an increase in the IMF. As described in section 2.4 the IMF has an
average strength around 7 nT. ICMEs have an average eld of 10.1 nT [7]. SIRs also cause an increase
of the magnetic eld with an average Bmax = 15.5 nT [8]. A limit of 9 nT is chosen as a reference for
disturbances. It is well above the "quiet" state of the solar wind, but it will still include many dierent
disturbances. The threshold is chosen by Susanne Vennerstrøm for the AWARE program. The same func-
tion findB_intervals.m from AWARE is used in this project to streamline the detection process. The
function is made for the hourly average data because the uctuations seen in minute data would give to
many short disturbances, that are normal uctuations. This was tested in the beginning and the conclusion
was that too many disturbances was detected compared to what was realistic based on the catalogues. It
is mainly because short peaks reaching just above 9 nT will be included. Between 1998-2008 the program
detected 807 disturbances in the minute average, but it was reduced to 717 detections for hourly average.
In the same period I. Richardson and H. Cane detected 286 ICMEs [7] and L. Jian detected 418 CIRs/SIRs
[8]. It is a total of 704 events which is similar to automated detection. One note is that some events in the
catalogues are hybrid event and thus categorised twice. Other conditions will also imply that the detection
rate is never one to one for the manual catalogues and the automated programs.
4.2
Initial investigation of OMNIweb data
The next important step is to visualise the data in a plot consisting of the relevant parameters. The plot
is separated in 4 horizontal parts with the same time axis. The graphs are magnitude of B, Bx & By, Bz
and SYM-H. Bz is plotted individually because it is important for the potential impact eciency. SYM-H
is equivalent to Dst, but is available every minute. The SYM-H data is not available in real time, but it
will be used as a measure for geomagnetic activity during the initial analysis. It can be used to see when
a geomagnetic storm is registered because it acts the same way as the Dst index does. The geomagnetic
storms are highlighted by magenta dots when SYM-H ≤−50 nT, where the limits are known from table
2.3. The disturbances are highlighted in all four parts by blue-shaded boxes. An example can be seen in
Figure 4.1. The visualisation is important in order to see features in the disturbances found by the program
while determining the best way to characterise the dierent types of disturbances. The characterisation is
also based on the theory described in chapter 2. No characterisation has been done in the plot in Figure
4.1. Even though magnetometers are less sensitive to extreme conditions, there are still data gaps. It is
about 5 % of the magnetic eld data, that is missing during longer time spans. The data gaps in the
magnetic data are still half of the plasma data. This is one of the benets of only using B. For the entire
year 2000 there is 5.9 % magnetic eld data missing, while the velocity data has a total data gap of 13.4%.
As the plot is analysed for several time periods, dierent criteria are considered for detecting ICMEs and
SIRs. Ideally, the criteria must distinguish the two types of events and still nd as many as possible in each
category. The most important events to nd are the geoecient ones, since it is the scope of the project.
==PAGE:47==
4.2 Initial investigation of OMNIweb data
31
Figure 4.1: Early plot of the chosen parameters (magnitude of B, Bx & By, Bz, and SYM-H & Dst) and
the disturbances, shown as blue boxes, found by the program
Therefore minimum of Bz is introduced as a criterion. It mainly targets ICMEs because the have larger
uctuations in Bz and are known to result in the most negative Bz extrema. As mentioned in section 2.5,
MCs are some of the most important events to nd since they can have long negative Bz. To make the
classication as precise as possible, other criteria are also included. Bmax is chosen because high magnetic
eld magnitude generally induces larger storms. This criterion is primarily targeted ICMEs, since they
have the highest Bmax values. Bmean is introduced because an overall high magnetic eld also shows high
potential of geoeectiveness. Then it is not just a sudden spike. The maximum peak values of the Bx and
By component are also considered to track the large uctuations some disturbances can give. There are
some challenges regarding this because both strong SIRs and ICMEs can show this behaviour. This will
be discussed more later.
As described in section 2.5 and 2.6, the behaviour of the B-component can be an eective feature for
identifying ICME, especially MCs, and SIR/CIRs apart. The reason is that the conditions of the magnetic
eld. MCs are known to have ux-rope structure, which gives smooth, slowly varying magnetic eld com-
ponents. Some ICMEs have MC-like behaviour and the criterion might detect some of them too, which is
a benet. SIRs on the other hand have a compressed magnetic eld which means it will uctuate a lot. If
the IMF of the compressed solar wind is strong, the uctuation are varying a lot and very fast. This is
a good motivation for using minute data. The dierence can be seen in the example of an ICME and a
SIR in appendix F. The ICME is an MC with smooth rotating magnetic eld after the peak, whereas the
magnetic eld components uctuate even more after the Bmax in the SIR.
To analyse the dierence between ux-rope structures and compressed and chaotic magnetic eld struc-
tures, the B-components are smoothed out by the built-in Matlab function smoothdata(x), where x is
the data vector. The function is doing a running average over a tting window. The window is determined
heuristically [72]. The smoothing is smart because a ux-rope structure will be close to the smoothed
version. To compare the smoothed data with the original data, two dierent methods are used. The rst
method is a simple subtraction of smoothed and real data for each data point within a found disturbance.
If the dierence is small, it means the data shows little uctuation. The other method is computing the
Root Mean Square Error (RMSE) with another function rsquare, that can be downloaded online [73].
The RMSE is the square root of the sum of squared value of the residuals, which are the dierence between
model and data. Both the dierence and RMSEx,y,z is used because it gives a better conrmation of the
behaviour. For both criteria the sum of the mean RMSEx,y,z and the sum of the mean of dierence in
smooth and real data are the values, which are evaluated for either high or low thresholds. They will also
highlight dierent events, which are useful, since as many events as possible must be categorised. The two
==PAGE:48==
32
Methodology
criteria can be used specically for MCs by setting the threshold to a low value and demand that the sum
of either criterion behave within this. For SIRs the sum must be above a certain threshold to track large
uctuations. Both criteria are tested twice for two time spans: from start to end, and from the peak of
B to the end. It is expected that MCs will show a better detection rate when using the second option,
since the sheath is where large uctuations might be observed and the peak located too. Thereafter the
ux-rupe structure is assumed dominate as seen in Figure 2.8. SIRs have chaotic magnetic elds during
the whole event.
Table 4.1 shows how the program works with the data. A set of threshold values is tried based on ob-
servations of the ICMEs found in the RC catalogue and the SIRs catalogue by I. Jian [8]. The aim is to
see how many disturbances are found and compare to the catalogues. Ideally they should all be repeated
there. The disturbances are tested if they are in the catalogue, by checking if the time of Bmax is within a
set of start and end times given in one of the catalogues. This is the most ecient approach, because the
start and end times are determined dierently in the program and in the catalogues. The peak should still
be within the events from the catalogue because it is the central part of both types of disturbances. The
geoeective ICMEs of the RC catalogue are found by selecting all those with a Dstmin ≤−50 nT. It is a
parameter given in the RC catalogue. This parameter is not given in the Jian catalogue and is therefore
not considered at the moment.
The rst column in table 4.1 describes the criterion and the chosen threshold, which is being tested until
the bold horizontal line.
The second column lists the number of events found by the program based
on the criterion in the respective row. The third column shows the percentage of the disturbances that
are repeated in the RC catalogue for each criteria.
The fourth column shows how large a percentage
disturbances distinguished by the criterion are repeated in the catalogue by RC for geoecient events.
The fth column shows how large a percentage of disturbances caught by the criteria are repeated in
Jian's catalogue for each criteria. Some of the spaces only show a line, which means the number is not
included due to irrelevance.
The last three rows sum up the performance of the above showed criteria by rst showing the total number
of events in the four categorises. Then in the second-last row, the total number of detected disturbances
and the number of those matched with each catalogue is displayed. The percentage describes how large
a part of the total number of detected disturbances are repeated in catalogue categories. Here it is clear
that the program will never detect all events in the solar wind, since the highest match is for the Jian
catalogue at 92.3 %. The last row displays all the unique disturbances caught by at least one criterion and
match to the three catalogue categorise respectively. Some of the criteria will highlight the same event,
which makes the sorting necessary. The number of unique disturbances detected by at least one criterion
is also matched to the total number of disturbances detected by the program in the rst space in the last
row. It is only 54.7% of the total number of detected disturbances that are caught by a criterion in this
analysis. This must be improved for later versions.

Methodology
The table shows that each criterion catches a very dierent number of disturbances. A positive eect of the
Bmax criterion is that it catches far more ICMEs than SIRs. This means that this parameter can target
ICMEs. It also shows that some SIRs do have very strong magnetic elds too. Bmean shows the same
tendencies as the Bmax criterion. The Bmean criterion does not catches a great number of disturbances
with the given threshold, but those, it catches, are geoecient which is very positive. Both the Bmean
and Bmax criteria have a matching rate over 100 % for the RC catalogue categories. This can happen if
two disturbances close to one another both include the peak of B time. A margin is also introduced to
the start and end time of 12 hours which makes this assumption more plausible. Since this is not the nal
version of the detection process, but an initial test, the "over-detection" is not investigated further. The
conclusion is still that the two criteria show good detection rates of ICMEs.
The Bzmin parameter catches 144 events.
Many of them are repeated in either the RC catalogue or
the Jian catalogue. It is positive that nearly 80% of the events repeated in the RC catalogue are also
geoeective. The threshold needs to be adjusted to target ICMEs more specically, since it is the purpose
of the criterion.
The Bzmax criterion has very similar results as the Bzmin, but it is not as interesting since it does not
drive geomagnetic storms. The small uctuations in Bx and By highlights a good amount of events, but
they do not match well with either of the catalogues. This means this criterion will likely not be considered
for future use. Some modication might enhance the performance.
The criteria analysing the uctuations of the B components show promising results. More disturbances are
matched in the RC catalogues when only the data after the peak is considered for the low threshold values
targeting MCs. The disturbances caught by the high thresholds targeting SIRs match the Jian catalogue
a little better when looking after the peak, but it is not a signicant increase. The SIR thresholds for
RMSE and dierence considerations are really ecient at detecting the right disturbances. The percentage
of correct hits is around 75%. The last three rows sum up the overall performance of the initial test.
The number of events found by the program is similar to the total number of events in the RC and Jian
catalogue. It is interesting to note that 73% of the ICMEs from the RC catalogue have a Dstmin ≤−50 nT.
The second to last row shows how many of the events found by the program by only looking at an increase
in the total B-eld is repeated in the catalogues. It is clear that not all events are found because they might
not result in an increase above 9 nT. This can happen in the quiet periods because the overall strength
of the events often is lower. The last row shows how many unique events are found by the program by
applying the presented criteria. Overall 54% of the events detected by the program are caught by one or
more criteria. The detection of ICMEs is a little better with 62 % caught. The detection of geoeective
ICMEs shows similar performance. A positive result is that this method caught 77% of the SIRs. This
will be considered later.
Overall this method has shown some interesting results. It is possible to detect disturbances with threshold
values.
For the next part of the process these thresholds will be considered since the background for
choosing them is manual investigation of events in the chosen time period. This is not robust enough
for the nal program. To see the dierent behaviour of ICMEs and SIRs, the catalogues are compared
in histograms for the next part. Also more criteria will be introduced to upgrade the performance. It is
mainly important to increase the detection of geoecient events. The dierence consideration and RMSE
criteria will also be selected to only work on the interval between the peak and the end because the best
results were obtained there. The criteria, looking at the uctuations in Bx and By, will also be considered
because they do not have a very good success rate here.
4.3
Determining the detection method
The results of the initial test in table 4.1 show that all thresholds of the dierent criteria will detect both
ICMEs and SIRs unless they are very extreme. To investigate this trade-o for the rst two criteria,
histograms of the values detected for the events in the RC catalogue and Jian catalogues are plotted in
Figure 4.2. The plot of the Bmax and Bmean distributions shows that the majority of events in both
catalogues peaks around the same values for some criteria. When looking towards the higher values, a
larger part becomes ICMEs as expected. ICMEs also accounts for the most extreme values. In this case
very extreme threshold for Bmax such as 40 nT would detect almost no SIRs, but the number of ICMEs
which meets this threshold is also very low. This defeats the purpose of categorising as many disturbances
as possible.
==PAGE:51==
4.3 Determining the detection method
35
Figure 4.2: Top: Histogram of Bmax for both catalogues. Bottom: Bmean for both catalogues.
In other parameters such as the sum of RMSE of the B-components there is a clear distinction between
the ICMEs of the RC catalogue and the SIRs of the Jian catalogue. The trade-o is still present though
since the side of each histogram overlap one another. It can be seen in Figure 4.3. Both criteria are useful
for categorising the disturbances.
Figure 4.3: Histogram showing the sum of the RMSE between smooth and real data for Bx, By & Bz for
events in the respective catalogues.
To meet the challenge of trade-o between catching only the desired category and catching many dis-
turbances, a score table is introduced for each category: ICME and SIR. The score table consists of all
the criteria considered relevant for each category. Then dierent threshold values are chosen based on
histograms like Figure 4.3, relevant theory and investigation of catalogued event in the data. For the rst
draft, 5 dierent scores are outlined, reaching from the most likely threshold of each criteria giving a score
of 5 while the least secure threshold gives a score of 1.
==PAGE:52==
36
Methodology
Before the score tables are created, a few more criteria are introduced. For ICME detection, shocks are
considered. For SIRs, polarity change in Bx or By is considered. The detecting procedure is described
below.
As described in section 2.5 shocks are manifested in the solar wind as sudden jumps in the magnetic eld
among other parameters. The procedure will look at jumps in B in the solar wind, where the magnitude
of the magnetic eld increases a certain amount. To verify that it is a shock and not just a peak in B,
two analyses are done. The rst is to check if the mean of the B in a selected period increases after the
jump compared to before. This is required for a shock. The other condition is that Bx, By and/or Bz
have to change simultaneously at the jump because the magnitude of B is based on all components. The
minute-average data is necessary when detecting shock, because the jump happens within minutes. A
investigation showed that is was a benet to compare data points, which were separated by a few minutes.
The required jump in B is set to 5 nT.
All data point of the entire time period with this jump are compared to the fth point before they are
saved for further analysis. Then the mean for 1 hour of B before and after the jump is calculated. It must
be an increase of 1/3 to be seen as a shock. Each point with a 5 nT jump is also tested for jump in Bx,
By and Bz simultaneously. The jump is required to happen in two out of three component and be of at
least 2.5 nT. The limit is less for the component because each one contributes to part of the total jump
in B. If either of these two conditions are fullled, it is considered a shock. In the end the shocks must be
coupled with an disturbance detected by the program. The time of the jump must be within one day of
the start of an event to be coupled. If yes a vector is saved indicating a 1 for shock, 0 for no shock for all
detected disturbances.
SIRs are known to originate from a coronal hole where the plasma streams out into the solar wind at high
speeds. The mean polarity of the magnetic eld components reects the polarity of the solar hemisphere
where the coronal hole is located. The general solar wind streams out from the helmet streamers around
equator where the mean polarity varies. A sudden change in the polarity between the ambient solar wind
and the disturbance, suggest a dierent origin which might be a coronal hole. This is the motivation for the
criterion. It is implemented by calculating the mean of the Bx and By component during the disturbance
and comparing it to a mean calculated for a 20 min interval one hour before the event starts. Since the
magnetic eld of SIRs and the ambient solar wind does not mix, one hour back tracing from the start is
enough. The program takes into account that the start time is registered a little later by the program
compared to reality, since B must exceed 9 nT to be detected. If a change in polarity is detected for either
Bx or By, then the disturbance get the assigned value 1 in a vector. If not then the disturbance is assigned
the value 0. This is done in two separate vectors for x and y. To achieve score points, a disturbance must
have 1 in one of the two vectors.
The criteria for the rst score tables are now ready. Each disturbance will be evaluated based on all criteria
and a nal score is given as the sum of all point from each criteria.
Then three dierent categories are determined: "ICME", "SIR" and "Unidentied". The disturbances are
categorised as "ICME" or "SIR" if the total score in the respective score table exceeds half of the total
possible score. Otherwise the disturbance is categorised as "unidentied". The score tables for ICMEs
and SIRs can be seen in table 4.2 and 4.3.
The performance of the score tables are evaluated in two ways. The most important method is the eval-
uation matrix, inspired by the contingency table from the article by M.D. Cash [40] where an automated
shock detection algorithm is the focus. It related the events found by the program to observed or veried
events. The columns are the dierent categories which are classied. The rows then show the number or
percentage of events categorised, matched in the RC catalogue and last in the Jian catalogue. The matrix
is constructed as seen in table 4.4. The Nhit describes the "true" events that are categorised correctly,
hence the match. Nfalse describes the "false positives", since they are categorised, but not matched in the
right catalogue. Nmiss is the events, which should have been categorised.
This draft of the score tables (table 4.3 and 4.2) has some challenges such as ve levels of score points,

which are unnecessary and too complicated. It is concluded that more categories could be an advantage
in order to include the events with ICME- or SIR-like features. The process is also expanded to take care
of events registered in both categories, which can happen for hybrid events. The repeated detections are
assigned to the category where they have the relative highest score compared to the maximum possible.
They are then removed from the other category. The number of nal categories are also extended from
three to ve, since some events show ICME or SIR-like behaviour, but are at the moment excluded because
they do not exceed a score of half the total score.
The evaluation matrix for the rst draft can be seen in chapter 5.
Some of the criteria for ICME detection are re-evaluated and two new criteria for SIR detection are in-
troduced: Parker spiral angle and relative time position of Bmax compared to the whole event interval.
The thresholds are evaluated based on histograms of the criteria of events registered in the Jian and RC
catalogue.
4.3.1
Next generation of score tables
The next generation of score tables have changed for both ICME and SIR categorisation. The Bz extrema
criterion is now separated in two for the ICMEs. This is because the negative Bz criterion is deemed very
important. For SIRs, two new criteria are introduced: the minimum Parker spiral angle and the relative
time position of Bmax. The process of determining these two will be described below before the new score
tables are presented. The next score tables include scores 1-3, where 3 is for the most certain threshold, 1
is for the least certain threshold.
A new criteria which can be a supplement to the SIR score table is the Parker spiral angle. If the angle is
little, it means the velocity is high, which is true for SIRs, especially the fast ones. The principle are seen
in Figure 2.7. This is not used for ICMEs because they do not follow the Parker spirals properly. At rst
an hourly average of Bx and By are computed for the event. Then the formula presented in section 2.4 is
used. The minimum and mean angle during each disturbance are calculated. Another necessary condition
for the Parker spiral angle during a SIR is that the angle must decrease because the velocity is always
increasing during a SIR. This can be seen in the example in appendix F in the velocity part. The rst
and last calculation of the angle in each disturbance are used to make a linear regression with the start
and end time. The steepness must be negative to be accepted in the score table. The thresholds are then
based on the minimum of the Parker angle.
As an additional parameter the relative time position of Bmax is considered. For SIRs it is expected to
be in the center near the SI. The time period from start to peak is compared to the full duration of the
event. This fraction must be around 0.5 to be a SIR. Three intervals will be dened around 0.5 for each
score level.
To better support the choice of threshold values a set of histograms are created comparing the two dierent
catalogues. This purpose is to see, which parameters will increase the chance of detection for the two
categories. During the evaluation it is considered that the start and end limit of each event in the catalogue
are based on other signatures then B. It means some parameters might act a little dierent, when they
are used in the program. The histograms will be included in the section 4.3.2, where the nal threshold
values for the last score tables are also plotted. The histograms are the same used here and later on.
The score tables 4.5 and 4.6 are the next version for the detection method. The thresholds are tested on the
OMNIweb data between 1998-2008. After each disturbance is given a score, 5 dierent categories are now
created: ICME, ICME-like, SIR, SIR-like and unidentied. The assigned category depends on the score in
the score table compared to the maximum possible score (excluding the yes/no criteria). To be categorised
==PAGE:55==
4.3 Determining the detection method
39
as a "true" category, the disturbance must score 2/3 of the possible score points. The "likeness" category
is for the disturbances with a score between 1/3 and 2/3 of the max score. The disturbances, with a score
below 1/3 of the max score in both score tables, are categorised as "unidentied".
As described in section 2.6, a stable coronal hole will create a SIR re-occurring every solar rotation for
possibly a very long time. About half of the SIRs are expected to return after 25 to 29 days. A pos-
sible way of detecting these are implemented in the code. The events categorised as SIR and SIR-like
are collected in one vector and sorted after time stamp. The time interval between one event and the
previous 5 events are calculated. If the time dierence is between 25 to 29 days then the two events are
extracted and the mean polarity in Bx and By respectively are compared and must be the same in order
to categorise it as a CIR. This is a requirement since the stream must origin from the same coronal hole.
It is dicult to assign more requirement to the disturbance because the magnetic signature can change
a lot from one occurrence to the next. The events categorised as CIRs are then removed from the SIR
and SIR-like events. The category will be tested and evaluated in the evaluation matrix for its performance.
The evaluation matrix is extended to include the three new categories: "ICME-like", "SIR-like" and "CIR".

4.3.2
Final version of score tables
After the rst test, the score tables are changed again based on the results, which can be seen in section
5.1. The thresholds are adjusted and some of the criteria have been removed and new are added. For
the ICME score table, the Bx and By extrema criteria have been removed because they are not based on
a physical principle and it is assumed to be a observational coincidence, not valid criteria. The criterion
based on small mean values of Bzmax and Bzmin is also removed, because, even though the histograms
show more separation for ICMEs and SIRs, it is self-contradicting. ICMEs are expected to have a higher
level of uctuation in Bz. The trailing part of the histograms might arise since the start and end time
determination for the catalogue is based manual observation whereas this program uses a threshold. The
low values are not expected to be repeated for this type of identication and thus is removed from the
score table. The criterion based on the time position of Bmax is implemented for the ICME detection too
because a histogram of the event from the Jian catalogue and RC catalogue showed signicant dierence
for the two types of events.
This can be seen in Figure 4.4.
The ICMEs generally have the peak in
the beginning of the event (the shock/sheath), where the magnetic eld is highly compressed behind the
shock front. The events registered having the peak at the end of the disturbance are not included as a
criterion. This behaviour is very unexpected and is most likely due to hybrid events where disturbances
merge together. It can give a peak late in the disturbance. It can also be ICMEs during the quiet period,
where the magnetic eld rises steadily during the event and then peaks before B decreases again, usually
much faster. The criterion based on the negativity of Bz has been given double score in each level (6, 4, 2)
because it is a priority to detect the geoecient disturbances. The max score used to the categorisation
has not been changed since the number of criteria is the same and a change would defeat the purpose of
increasing the score points.
Figure 4.4: Histogram of the relative time position of Bmax during disturbance with ICME and SIR
thresholds.
For the SIR score table, the Parker spiral angle criterion has been changed to work with the mean spiral
angle during the event instead of the minimum. This is chosen to make the detection less sensitive to
sudden spikes in the velocity, which can give a very low Parker spiral angle, but it will not give the correct
interpretation of the whole event. The mean of the Parker spiral angle indicates if the velocity generally is
high. Therefore the thresholds have been changed to match the histogram showing the mean angle. The
score points of the criterion analysing the polarity change before and inside an event has been changed to
2, since it was determined to be better and more ecient for categorising correctly. Otherwise it carried
to much weight compared to its level of certainty.
==PAGE:58==
42
Methodology
The thresholds are based on histograms over the events found in the RC and Jian catalogues.
The
histograms that have been used throughout the construction of the score tables can be seen here, where
the nal threshold values are plotted. The threshold used for the ICME score table are plotted in blue
and those for the SIR score table are plotted in green. The bold line marks the 3 score points, the dashed
line marks the 2 score points and the narrow, dotted line marks the 1 score point. This gives an idea of
the chosen trade-o between detecting the right category and including as many disturbances as possible.
Figure 4.5: Histogram of Bmean and Bmeax for both catalogues including threshold for ICME classication.
Figure 4.6: Histogram showing the minimum of Bz and thresholds for ICME classication.
The histograms are used to get a better estimation of eective threshold values and the general behaviour
of SIRs and ICMEs. In Figure 4.5 the upper graph shows the Bmax values. Both the majority of SIRs
and ICMEs have a Bmax around 12 nT. When looking at the upper end of the histograms, it is clear that
ICMEs carry the highest peaks. Hence it can be used as a criterion. Above 25 nT almost every event is
a ICME. For Bmean in the lower graph, the same pattern is visible. Above 20 nT most events are ICMEs
too.
In Figure 4.6 minimum of Bz in all events from the two catalogues are plotted. It shows that the majority

of ICMEs have a lower value of Bzmin than SIRs. On the other hand the ICMEs also accounts for most
extrema values. Below -20 nT it is mostly ICMEs. The threshold might be set less negative, since the aim
is to nd the geoeective event and then some false detections will be accepted.
The sum of the dierence between the smooth and real data for all three components of B shows very

can be very chaotic too.

Methodology
Table 4.9: Categories based on scores.
Category
Required score
ICME
ICME score ≥12
ICME-like

The max scores are 18 for ICME score table and 12 for SIR score table. In the nal edition of the program,
the CIR detection has been removed again. Results from the performance evaluation, presented in the
next chapter, show that the CIRs found by the program in the OMNIweb data between 1998 to 2008
are matches almost equally with the RC and Jian catalogue. The method is also unable to identify more
than 16 % of the SIR and SIR-like events as CIRs. This is too low. Due to time issues the CIRs will be
omitted for the rest of the project, but the categorisation of them will be included as a part of future work
suggestions in section 7.
The performance is also analysed by comparing the distribution of values within each criteria for cate-
gorised event and those that have been matched to the relevant catalogue. It makes it possible to see a
dierence in the categorised events and evaluate the thresholds.
The primary task of this program is to categorise disturbances in the solar wind. The most important
of those are the disturbances that might cause geomagnetic storms.
To evaluate how geoeective the
categorised ICMEs and SIRs are, the geoeectiveness is calculated. The result is then compared to that
of the ICMEs in the RC catalogue and the SIRs in Jian's catalogue. The geoeectiveness is simply the
number of events causing a geomagnetic disturbance on Earth (Dst ≤−50 nT) compared to the total
number of events observed [57]. The test will be done for the OMNIweb data in the test period and the
validation period.
An important part of this project is to estimate the potential risk of an incoming disturbance. In this
project the Dst index is estimated based on a linear relation between the minimum of Bz and the minimum
of the Dst index. The relation is given in the literature [7]. To test if the relation is well-dened for all
disturbances too, a linear regression has been done between the minimum of Bz and SYM-H, which
correspond directly to Dst. The relation is then tested for all disturbances and only for those categorised
as ICME/ICME-like. The idea is to test if it works for all type of disturbances or it is better for ICMEs
as literature suggests [7].
To estimate the minimum Dst, which can be expected for a disturbance, the linear relation presented by
Richardson and Cane [7] will be implemented. This relation is chosen because the catalogue serves as
reference for the ICME categorisation. The relation will be used for all events which is not ideal since it
was designed for ICMEs. The decision is made because the dependence of Dst on Bz is expected to be
similar no matter what disturbance hits Earth even though ICMEs have the best relation between those.
It is also a very simple method which is benecial. Also the estimated Dst must serve as an indicator for
potential impact. The real observation are conducted by the WDC for Geomagnetism in Kyoto, Japan.
An inclusion of their real-time results in the real-time program will be considered later on. The potential
wrong estimates are accepted for the estimation of Dstmin, because it is a very simple way to get an
indication for how the disturbance will impact Earth.
Finally a table is created and saved as output. It includes the columns:
 Start year, day and time - given as: year, month/day, hour:minute.
 End (year), day and time - given as (year,) month/day, hour:minute, year is only included if the
event takes place over new years eve.
 Bmax - maximum of B during the event, in nT.
 Bzmin - given in nT. This is important for the potential geoeciency.
==PAGE:63==
4.4 Validation based on STEREO A and B data
47
 Estimated Dst - the Dst value for the Bzmin is calculated based on the linear relation from Richardson
and Canes article describing the catalogue: Dst = 8.23 · Bz + 3.74 [7].
 Shock detection - the column indicates if a shock is detected (Y) or not (N).
 RMSEx,y,z - sum of the mean of RMSE for the magnetic components during the disturbance.
 Sum of the mean of the dierence between smooth and real data for the magnetic components during
the disturbance.
 Category - the column tells which category the event is sorted in. It can be ICME, ICME-like, SIR,
SIR-like or unidentied based on the score in the score tables.
 Catalogue match - the column indicates if the event is matched by an event in the Jian catalogue for
SIR, SIR-like and CIR events or Richardson and Canes catalogue for ICME and ICME-like events.
 Warning - the column indicate whether or not a warning is needed. If the estimated Dst is below
-50 nT, a warning must be issued (Y), if not it is here noted as a no (N).
A nal plot is also generated with highlight of the categorised events. The ICME-category events are
in the same colour (cyan), whereas the "true" events are less transparent. The same is the case for the
SIR-category events (green). An example can be seen in Figure 4.10. Many disturbances are classied, but
there are still some that are unidentied (grey). It is more critical when they drive a geomagnetic storm,
which can be seen in the estimated Dst index. Some of the disturbances resulting in Dst below -50 nT
(magenta dots) are not surrounded by two lines (red and black for start and end), but it is the minority.
The nal version of the program used to test and validate the OMNIweb data is included in appendix G.
Figure 4.10: An example of the nal plot output from Jan-Jun 2001.
4.4
Validation based on STEREO A and B data
The score tables will be validated on STEREO A and B data too and the performance is investigated
to see how it matches the catalogued events from the chosen period and the OMNIweb validation. The
evaluation matrix is used for considering the performance of the program. The results are expected to
be similar to OMNIweb data from the validation period. Before the program is applied to the data, a
reference catalogue is downloaded for SIRs and ICMEs found in the STEREO data. Both lists are edited
to be compatible with Matlab. The data from STEREO are available between 2006 and 2014 for both
==PAGE:64==
48
Methodology
satellites. After this only STEREO A is working. Due to the launch data in late 2006, only data from
2007 and onwards are considered here.
The evaluation matrix of the validation will be presented in the next chapter.
A qualitative investigation of the correlation between disturbances observed in STEREO B, L1 and
STEREO A is conducted. The purpose is to see how the disturbances develop and consider if it would be
possible to predict the arrival time and strength of SIRs due to their movement along the Parker spiral in
magnetic eld data. The STEREO spacecraft can be used to this investigation since they at some point
in their orbit are placed favourably relative to the Earth. In 2008 the position is favourable, since they are
30° HEE (Earth Ecliptic) longitude from Earth on both sides.
The program used for validating the STEREO data is included in appendix H.
4.5
DSCOVR implementation
After the method is tested on old data, the next step in the project is to apply the detection and categori-
sation program to real time data. In order to do so, DSCOVR data is accessed online where the 7 day le
is opened in Matlab for further analysis. Matlab has a build-in function webread(url) which opens the
data in a cell. A few adjustments to the data are necessary before it is analysed, plotted and information
about the latest disturbances are saved.
Since only minute-average data is available for 7 days, one of the rst steps is to make an hour-average
and apply the function to detect disturbances. The hourly average of Bz is also used to calculated the
estimated Dst index since it is also calculated for every hour. Possible Dst below -50 nT is also located
for the chosen time period. It will be used later for the plotting. The hourly average of Bx and By are
needed for the Parker spiral analysis too.
More parameters relevant for the plot is dened before the disturbance dependent code because they are
necessary for the plot in any case. The analysis and the following categorisation of the disturbances is the
exact same as used for the OMNIweb and STEREO data. The only dierence is the opening of the data
and the plotting in the end.
Some changes are necessary, when the data amount is reduced from several years to only 7 days. One of
the primary challenges was to handle periods with no disturbances in the B-eld or a limited number of
disturbances. A if-statement in the beginning of the code makes sure that the extensive data analyses is
only carried out if an event is detected. The analysis is only relevant for categorisation of events.
In the end, the data is plotted with the four graphs deemed relevant: total B, Bx & By, Bz and the
estimated Dst index based on Bz.
If a disturbance is detected, the data analysis computes and extract the relevant data for the score tables.
In the end each event is tested in the score tables and based on the score, a category is assigned. To make
the separation of each category more clear 5 dierent colours are used in the plot. They are the following:
A few other changes have been implemented for the code to handle between one and four events per week.
It is primarily in the categorisation part after the ICME and SIR score tables. The normal occurrence
rate is 1-4 events for a week. All ve category vectors must have a length equal to the number of possible
disturbances, but the components remains undetermined for the components larger than the number of
detected disturbances. Based on category vectors, a cell array containing the relevant labels are created.
It is important for the plot and the output table. In the end, the plotting sequence is divided in two; one
for no disturbances and one including the categorised events.
It was tested if the result could be compared to the category determined by AWARE automatically.
Unfortunately it is not possible to access the AWARE data online through Matlab, since a login is required
before the data can be obtained. It is not possible to avoid this at the moment. It will be considered as
a feature for future programs if ESA changes their protocol. In this project the comparison will be done
manually for a few examples.
==PAGE:65==
4.5 DSCOVR implementation
49
The behaviour of the estimated Dst will also be compared to the observed Dst published by the WDC
for Geomagnetism in Kyoto.
Then the proles will be commented on to evaluated the behaviour of
the estimated Dst index.
The data is available online here: http://wdc.kugi.kyoto-u.ac.jp/dst_
realtime/lastmonth/index.html.
In the end an output table is constructed in the program and saved on the computer with the parameters
 Start date and time
 End date and time
 Maximum of B
 Minimum of Bz
 Estimated minimum of Dst
 Shock detection (Y/N)
 RMSEx,y,z
 Sum of the mean of the dierence in smooth and real data
 Estimated mean velocity (only for SIR/SIR-like events)
 Category assigned: "ICME", "ICME-like", "SIR", "SIR-like" or "unidentied"
 Warning issued - Dstmin < −50 nT (Y/N)
 Data gap during the 7 day period, /%
The nal code for real-time analysis of DSCOVR data is included in appendix I.
At the moment the Sun is close to the minimum between solar cycle 24 and the coming solar cycle 25.
Therefore there are very few disturbances and they are not that powerful. In order to see how the program
will react to dierent types and strengths of disturbances, simulated real time is applied.
It is done
by using DSCOVR data from 2016-2017 and only opening 7-days periods one at a time. The program
then analyses the given period before the time interval is moved half a day ahead. Then the analysis
is done once again and disturbances are categorised again independently of the previous run. This way
the entire development of a disturbance can be investigated. It also makes it possible to see how robust
the categorisation is. In the beginning and end of the 7-day interval the program is expected to give
uncertain results, but as long as the whole event is clearly visible, the program should not be aected
by the changing time period. The continuous analysis of a time period with updates of half a day allows
for better investigation of the performance of the program. It shows the changes over time which are
really important. This will be clear in chapter 5. The disturbances, which are detected will once again be
compared to events from the catalogue by I. Richardson of ICMEs. It is the continuation of the catalogue
used previously. Since the catalogue by I. Richardson only includes ICMEs, the list of all disturbances
and their respective classication detected by AWARE is used. It was kindly put at my disposal for this
project by Susanne Vennerstrøm. It includes detections from 1995 to 2017.
Further development consideration will be done based on this investigation together with other experiences
during the process.
==PAGE:66==
 
==PAGE:67==
5
|
Results
The results of the various steps in developing, testing and validating the program designed to categorise
disturbances in the solar wind and estimate the risk will be presented in this chapter. The results are
divided into sections describing and highlighting the most signicant observations. The development from
one step to another will be outlined and accompanied by illustrations from the program. The discussion
of the results will be a part of chapter 6.
The rst section sums up the program development made with the OMNIweb data from 2008-1998. The
primary task here is to develop the categorisation method. The existing catalogues by Richardson & Cane
[7] and Jian [8] have been crucial for developing the method as trusted reference points.
5.1
Program development results
As described in chapter 4 the results will be investigated in two part: the evaluation matrix in table 4.4
and histograms comparing ICMEs and SIRs categorised by the program.
The rst draft of the ICME and SIR score tables (table 4.5 & 4.6) dened 3 dierent categories, which are
evaluated in the matrix designed for this. The percentage tells how large a part of the detected disturbances
in that category in the column is matched with the two catalogues in the last rows. The categorisation
overview of the rst score tables can be seen in table 5.1
Table 5.1: Evaluation matrix for test of drafts for score tables

The matrix shows that almost 60 % of the event categorised as ICMEs are matched in the RC catalogue. It
shows that the method can identify these events with reasonable success. A disadvantage is that nearly 20
% of the events are matched in Jian's catalogue of SIRs too. The percentage of false positives are similar
for the SIR category, but the number of true matches are higher by 10 percentage points. One of the
problems, which can be highlighted for this version of the score tables, is that the number of unidentied
events is higher than the total number of categorised events (172+169 = 341). The next version of the score
tables must aim at reducing the number of unidentied disturbances. This is the reason for introducing
the "likeness" categories, because they allow the classication to include more events, which have features
know from the ICMEs or SIRs.
As the score tables are updated, the second test results from the OMNIweb data analysis is presented
in table 5.2 and 5.3. Table 5.2 shows the results when the CIR detection is included in the program.
There are some problems with the method since only 60 CIRs are categorised. It is expected that the
SIR/CIR ratio is approximately 50/50, which means that the program is not successful in categorising the
CIRs. Also they are matched almost equally often with events from the Jian and RC catalogue. These
problems motivated the decision of removing the CIR detection from the nal version of the program.
The result from the second test can be seen in table 5.3. The detection rate of ICMEs is nearly the same

with or without the CIR category. Without CIRs a higher number of events from the ICME-like category
are registered in the Jian catalogue. This can happen if some of the events categorised as CIRs, now
are wrongly categorised in the ICME-like category. Otherwise both the number of categorised SIRs and
SIR-like events are increased, which is expected since the CIR events are detected based on these. The
percentage of SIR and SIR-like events matched in the Jian catalogue is increased when the CIR category
is removed. The number of unidentied disturbances have decreased a lot with the new score table, which
is very positive. 1/4 of the unidentied disturbances are matched in the RC catalogue whereas 37 % are
matched in the Jian catalogue when the CIRs included. The percentage are increased, when the CIRs are
removed, to 54 %. The unidentied disturbances, which are not in any of the catalogues, are sometimes
normal uctuations in the solar wind and it is expected to occur.

The ratio of positive hits are high for all four categories, where ICME-like events show the best score. The
number of false positives are below 25% for all categories too and even equal to or below 20% for three
out of four categories. This is an improvement compared to the last test version seen in table 5.3. The
number of categorised ICMEs and ICME-like disturbances are approximately halved. The issue might be
the conict between detecting MC and non-MCs. The lower detection rate can also be due to the decrease
in number of criteria. The excluded criteria had some benets and they did contribute to the number of
categorised disturbances, but they were not physically substantiated and therefore not applicable. The
number of unidentied is increased with four percentage point from ≈26% of the total number of events
in the second test version without CIRs to ≈30% for the nal version.
Some of the criteria are analysed after the categorisation to see if they match the expectations from the
initial histograms. The sum of RMSE and smooth and real data dierence showed the same tendency as
expected, which can be seen in Figure 5.1. The histogram also shows that the matched events are evenly
distributed over all values. It means that a specic part cannot be excluded to increase the number of
hits.

5.2. As expected the ICMEs have the lowest values in Bz. The lowest value registered are around −60 nT
which will result in an estimated Dst of −490 nT. The ICME/ICME-like histogram is more at than

The Parker spiral angle is a direct method to estimate the velocity of SIRs because of their propagation
behaviour as describes in section 2.4. The relation between the magnetic eld components in the ecliptic

disturbances gives an idea of the behaviour. The distributions can be seen in Figure 5.3. The minimum

there are slow SIRs too. The mean angle distribution shows the general behaviour where the distribution
is centred around 45° as expected. There is a slight overweight of the mean values towards the higher

Table 5.5 shows the geoeectiveness of ICMEs and SIRs found in the catalogues by Richardson & Cane
[7] and Jian [8] and those found and categorised by the nal version program. The Dst values given in the
OMNIweb data are used for the geoeectiveness analysis because it is more precise than the estimated Dst
index. The ICMEs are the most geoeective as expected, where the program has a higher ratio of events
causing a storm. This is positive since the aim is to nd the potentially dangerous events. In general
38.4 % of the total number of the events found by the program results in a moderate (Dst ≤−50 nT)
storm, 13 % results in a strong (Dst ≤−100 nT) storm and 3% would result in a severe (Dst ≤−200 nT)
storm. This means that in the 11 year period around a maximum 19 events can have serious consequences
on Earth. The number of severe storms is not constant over many solar cycles, but this proves that the
dangerous geomagnetic storms are not that rare.

As a last investigation of the data, the linear relation between the Bzmin and SY MHmin is computed for
two dierent cases. Remembering that SYM-H is equivalent to Dst just recorded every minute, the Bzmin
of each disturbance is expected to be somewhat linearly correlated with the resulting SY MHmin orDstmin.
At rst all detected disturbances are included. This t gives the relation: SY MHmin = 6.77Bzmin +28.24
(R2 = 0.63) and can be seen in Figure 5.4. Afterwards only the events categorised as ICMEs and ICME-like
are used. It can be seen in Figure 5.5, where the linear t is: SY MHmin = 7.30Bzmin +14.72 (R2 = 0.71).
The correlation coecient is higher for the ICME categorised events only as expected. The coecient in
this relation is closer to the values presented by I. Richardson and H. Cane in their study [7], but their
correlation coecient is higher. This test conrms that that the relation exists and is easy to obtain with
relatively high correlation coecient. The best results are obtained with large data sets to give more data

The evaluation matrix in table 5.6 shows that the categorisation method is less ecient for the validation
interval. It is true for all categories. It is positive that the percentage of false positives is at the same level.
The decrease in the percentage of hits proves that the thresholds chosen in the criteria used in the test
phase are over-tted to that period of time. It can be concluded since there is a decrease in all categories.
The percentages of hits in the Jian catalogue for SIR-like event is 74.12 % which is similar to the test
interval. In general the "true" events are less likely to match the catalogues than the likeness events, but
the true events cause fewer false positives. The reason for the fewer number of hits can be due to the
change in the strength of the solar cycle. It can be seen in Figure 2.3, where cycle 23 is more intense than
24. If the histogram of the results for the minimum of Bz in Figure 5.6 is compared to the test results in
Figure 5.2, it can be seen that the distribution is narrower. It is true for both the ICME categories and
SIR categories. The general distribution of the values are the same. The ICME/ICME-like histogram has
a at distribution, while the SIR/SIR-like distribution is centred around a peak. The events matched in
the catalogues are still evenly distributed.
Figure 5.6: Histogram showing the Bzmin of ICMEs and SIRs in the validation period 2009-2014.

and mean of B during events being lower. The histograms for these can be seen in appendix E. For SIR/SIR-
like events the dierence is especially clear for the Parker spiral angle. In Figure 5.7, the distribution of
the minimum observations are more widely spread out towards the higher values. The mean value of the
angle peaks around 55° for this period. It clearly shows that the expected velocity observations are lower,
which supports the observations of a less intense solar cycle.

Geoeectiveness is as mentioned, how many of the registered events in a time period, that result in a
geomagnetic storm. The geoeectiveness of SIRs and ICMEs are calculated for the validation period and
the results can be seen in table 5.2. The categorisation by the program is compared to the catalogues to
see how they compare.
The geoeectiveness of both ICMEs and SIRs is lower in the validation period, which can be explained by
the validation period primarily being during less intense maximum and declining phase than the previous
solar cycle. The ICMEs have a higher geoeectiveness than SIRs, which is expected. The geoeectiveness
of the ICMEs categorised by the program is higher than for the catalogue. It can be explained, since
more low intensity events are included in the total number in the catalogue than in the program due
to the limit of 9 nT for a disturbance detection in the program. Once again the program has a higher
geoeectiveness of the detected and categorised disturbances.
The geoeectiveness of the unidentied
events is 25%, which is a little to high. It still means that most of the unidentied events are not likely to
cause a geomagnetic storm. The level of geoeectiveness for the unidentied events is also nearly constant
for the test and validation period. There are no severe storms registered in this period, but 5% are strong
storms (Dstmin ≤100 nT).
