The STEREO data shows a lower rate of categorisation overall, which is very interesting. The time period
of 6 years is similar to that of the validation of OMNIweb data. The total number of detections are also
similar. The number of SIR and SIR-like events are surprisingly low for both STEA and STEB, which
suggests that the method is not optimal for detecting SIRs in this period of time or some error has oc-
curred. The latter is must likely since it worked as expected for the OMNIweb validation. This once again
conrms that the program is over-tted to the initial time interval of OMNIweb data. It is surprising that
a larger part of the ICME-like events are matched in the SIR catalogue and not the ICME catalogue. This
supports the theory of an error in the categorisation or matching process. Some of the disturbances are
not caught properly in the data. A re-evaluation of the threshold values, e.g. making them depending on
the previous detections might improve the results, since it might increase the rate of categorisation and
minimise errors.
The number of unidentied events is still high, but it is mostly matched in the SIR catalogue or not at all.
Very few disturbances are caught in both "true"-categories, which suggest that the thresholds are too high
or excluding to many disturbances for this period. There are almost 50/50 of categorised and unidentied
disturbances, which is to high for the latter.
Sometimes STEA and STEB are in a position that makes it favourable for trying to predict incoming
SIRs to L1 because they move with the Parker spirals as described in section 2.6. The satellites observing
the data collected in OMNIweb are located in or near L1, which will be the reference for the following
summary. As described in chapter 3, OMNIweb data is shifted to the bow shock nose in the nal output.
In 2008 STEA and -B are approximately 30° apart from Earth on either side in the ecliptic plane. It
means that STEB will encounter the SIRs rst and STEA will observe the event after the satellites in L1.
A small investigation of a short period in 2008 is carried out to see how the data changes over time. As
described earlier, disturbances can change surprisingly much over a short time. It will also be considered
why STEREO data has so many events categorised as ICME-like, when they are SIR/SIR-like in OMNI-
web data. 2008 is close to minimum between the 23rd and 24th solar cycle. Therefore mostly SIRs are
expected to be observed.
The three following images in Figure 5.8 show observations from July to September 2008 displayed for
STEB, L1 and STEA respectively, as it is the order of encounter. STEA and -B both show more distur-
bances than the OMNIweb data. The peaks and the general intensity level of the data are higher. This
suggests that the data processing is dierent. The solar wind is not stronger in any direction on an average
basis. Therefore the intensity of the observations is expected to be the same for all three spacecraft, but
the observed dierence explains why there are more disturbances for STEA and -B. If the velocity of the
SIRs in general is expected to be 500 km/s and the radius of Earth orbit is assumed constant and equal
to 1 AU (149.6 mio. km), it gives a circumference of
CEarth = (1.496 × 1011 m) · 2π = 9.4 × 1011 m
==PAGE:75==
5.3 Validation results for STEREO A and B data
59
which can be used to nd a time shift. Since 30° is 1/12 of the total circumference, it is assumed that the
solar wind moves in a straight line between Earth and STEREO A or B. It is then divided by the assumed
speed to calculate the time dierence. This time shift changes with velocity, so high speed streams will
shorten it further.
tshift =
CEarth/12
500 × 103 ms−1 = 1.57 × 105 s = 43.5 h
(5.1)
There are four dierent events found by the program for OMNIweb data. They are all categorised as
SIR-like. If the AWARE list is consulted, the three rst events are categorised as SIRs and the last is
SIR-like. All four events result in estimated Dst values below -50 nT, meaning the can drive geomagnetic
storms. The rst disturbance in the OMNI data correspond to the double event in the beginning of the
STEB data. The delay of almost two days ts quite well here. The rst peak in STEB has a value close to
15 nT, which is the same as the OMNIweb data. Further inspection could suggest that several peaks have
merged into one event before reaching L1. In STEA the disturbance is the second one in the graph. It
is now undened and single-peaked. The maximum value has decreased to around 10 nT, which indicates
that the SIR is losing some strength. The third and fourth disturbances from STEB are not detected in
the OMNIweb data. There is a peak shortly after the 21st of July in L1, but it is not intense enough to
be detected. Midway between the 21st and 31st of July, a disturbance is detected for STEA, which the
repetition of the one from STEB. It is possible that the SIR can vary in strength, since the peak is around
10 nT. The length is also similar, but it is strange that two disturbances seem to be merged or one is gone
between STEB and STEA.
The second peak in the OMNI data is just before the 10th of August. Bmax = 18 nT which is the same for
the unidentied event in STEB approximately 2 days before. Both disturbances are relatively short with
a high peak. Strangely, it is not seen in the STEA data. This once again emphasizes how dynamic these
structures are. The sixth disturbance in the STEB data shows a similar structure as the previous one, but
the peak is higher. It is also categorised as ICME-like. A rise in B is seen in OMNIweb just before the
20th of August, but it is not powerful enough to be detected. The ICME-like disturbance is one again seen
in STEA. This must be a coincidence because the disturbance at the 15th of August is the only ICME
observed in the STEB data int that period. Around the 1st of September a disturbance with generally
high B values is detected in L1. The corresponding event in the STEB data shows a more steep rise and
fall in the B values. STEA shows a disturbance with generally high B eld too, but a second disturbance
occurs right after, which is not seen in the two previous data sets. It is due to an ICME, which hits STEA
on the 4th of September. The second peak is the repetition of the SIR. Another ICME is catalogued for
the STEA data on the 5th of July, but it is categorised as a SIR-like event instead (rst disturbance in
the plot). The last event in OMNI shows similar structure in both STEA and STEB, but it is unidentied
here, whereas it is correctly determined to be a SIR in L1. This categorisation is conrmed in the AWARE
list.
This small investigation of 3 months of data shows great variation between the three satellites. In this
period, where a lot of the disturbances are around the threshold of 9 nT for detection, the number of found
events varies a lot. It is still uncertain why most of the disturbances in STEA and STEB are categorised
as ICME/ICME-like. The number of detection in STEREO B and A is conrmed by the SIR catalogue
for STEREO, which supports the idea that the main problem is in the categorisation.
It is possible to follow the development of dierent events through the time period, where the dynamic
behaviour of the solar wind is obvious. The B-eld changes a lot over small distances and short time
periods, where other plasma parameters might be more stable. This will be important for future space
applications trying to predict SIRs from an upstream position such as L5. If the period January to March
the same year is considered, it can be seen that the behaviour is the same as seen in Figure 5.9. All ex-
cept two disturbances are categorised as ICME-like in STEB, where as the OMNIweb analysis categorises
the disturbances as SIR/SIR-like, if not unidentied. Consolidation of AWARE's list shows that the rst
two events in January are unidentied and the next three in February and March are SIRs for L1. The
categorisations assigned by this program on the OMNIweb data agrees to some extend. The two methods
disagree on the rst and third disturbance. STEREO disagrees in both the number of disturbances and in
the category. Once again it is seen that most disturbances are categorised as ICME-like, and there are eight
disturbances detected. If the STEREO SIR catalogue is consulted, it tells that 7 SIRs are registered and
these are detected by the program with very high success rate. The categorisation is wrong though. The
plot from STEREO in both Figure 5.8 and 5.9 shows that the wrong categorisation is a general problem,
which is supported by the results from the evaluation matrices 5.9 and 5.8.

All in all the detection rate of the disturbances in the STEREO data is acceptable, when compared to
the catalogues. The challenge arises when the categorisation analysis is considered. Something is clearly
wrong since the majority of the disturbances are categorised incorrectly and the error is systematic. If

for future work on this program, since it is build for L1 data.

5.4
Results of real-time program
The real-time program has been constructed to produce a plot of the recent 7 days and a table including
useful information about the detected events during that time after the analysis.
The detecting and
categorisation method is still the same as used for the OMNIweb and STEREO data, but the opening of
data has changed which can bes seen in appendix I. An example can be seen in Figure 5.10 and table 5.10.
There are highlighted three dierent events in the period between the 13th to 20th of August 2018. The
last is still developing and the category might therefore change. The second, very short event might extend
longer if more observational data parameters are considered, but the large uctuations in the B-eld causes
the program to choose start and end time just on each side of the peak. Manual investigation suggests
that the disturbance begins around the 16th of August at 08:00. Since the disturbance is halved by the
automated detection, the categorisation is also more dicult. It is a separate event though because the
B-eld falls to the normal level around midnight between the 15th and 16th of August after the previous
disturbance. It is just before the next peak is occurring. The peaks are generally low, with the highest
value just above 12 nT.
For the rst and last event, it seems as if the peak registered is not at the
maximum value of B in the displayed data. This is a consequence of using the hourly average to detect
the disturbances including the peak of B. The seemingly highest point might be smoothed out when the
running mean is constructed and then transferred to minute data.
Figure 5.10: 7-days plot of real time magnetic eld data from DSCOVR with disturbances categorised by
the program.
The best way to validate the categorisation is to look at AWARE's results. The same detection method
is applied to both programs, but since AWARE uses both magnetic eld data and plasma data, it is
assumed to be more precise. A 3 day plot of the data from AWARE can be seen in Figure 5.11 as an
example. AWARE has categorised the events as a CIR, which means the two programs agree to some
extend. AWARE does not have a clear distinction between SIR and CIR. The program from this project
has only categorised it as a SIR-like events.
It is not possible to compare the previous events, since
AWARE only publishes the recent detections online, if the list is not obtained from Susanne Vennerstrøm,
who is the primary developer. AWARE can be accessed here after asking for permission to gain access
http://swe.ssa.esa.int/heliospheric-weather.
Another example shows that AWARE are better to categorise events in some cases even though they have
clear magnetic structures. This can be seen for the MC taking place between the 25/8 to 26/8 2018 which
can be seen in Figure 5.12 and the same observations from AWARE in Figure 5.13, where the event is
an ICME. The disturbance is clearly a MC, since the magnetic eld component shows low uctuations
and smooth, rotating behaviour. The MC in unipolar with a very negative Bz at its minimum. It means
a geomagnetic storm will impact Earth. The estimated minimum of Dst is ≈−130 nT. The WDC for

Geomagnetism and Space Magnetism in Kyoto responsible for the Dst index registered a minimum of
Dstmin,obs = −169 nT. In this case, the program underestimates the Dst index. The Dst index observa-
tions published by WDC for geomagnetism during August 2018 is display in Figure 5.14. It is interesting
to compare the Dst prole from the real observations to the estimated ones. The real observations show
a sudden and steep fall in Dst, where as the estimated Dst falls slowly towards the minimum, since it
directly follows the Bz component. The restoration phase also shows a more steep rise towards the normal
level again in the observations. This investigation suggests that the Dst index react more suddenly to the
changes, which means that the estimated Dst can give a warning beforehand because it reacts as soon as
Bz goes negative. The time of the minimum is close to one another, but the estimated Dst reaches critical
level before the real data, as described.
The wrong categorisation of the disturbance in the late August is likely due to the peak of B being close
to the end. It means that the RMSE and dierence criteria will not give any score points for ICME, but
likely will for SIR, since there are some trailing uctuations. This deviate from the expected behaviour
which might be due to the solar minimum and then lack of shock front. The MCs and ICMEs here do not
propagate with very high velocity, which leads to absence of shock and the following sheath region. This
means that the peak happens later in the disturbance. The maximum of B and minimum of Bz are also
less extreme than the ICMEs seen near maximum. This is one of the challenges for the program since it
has a better detection rate at the moment during the maximum period with higher intensity events. The
extreme conditions makes it easier for the threshold to categorise the right disturbances.

5.5 Results of simulated real-time investigation

5.5
Results of simulated real-time investigation
Since the possibilities of validating the real time categorisation are limited a dierent data set up is in-
troduced. Older data from DSCOVR is opened as 7 day periods moving half a day ahead for each loop.
In this way the program can be tested for a longer time span to see the behaviour of the analysis on
disturbances emerging, developing and exiting the active time period.
As reference, the ICME catalogue by Ian Richardson publish on the website http://www.srl.caltech.
edu/ACE/ASC/DATA/level3/icmetable2.htm#* is used. It is an extension of the catalogue presented in
the article "Near-Earth Interplanetary Coronal Mass Ejections During Solar Cycle 23 (1996-2009): Catalog
and Summery of Properties" [7]. It was lastly updated the 6th of June (20-08-18).
A few examples will be presented in the following to illustrate the behaviour of relevant disturbances during
the simulated real-time.
The rst example shows an ICME that is recognised by the program as soon as it enters the time span. It
can be seen in Figure 5.15.
Figure 5.15: ICME emerging and easily being categorised due to the magnetic eld structure. A strong
geomagnetic storm is also happening.
The ICME showed in Figure 5.16 is a very nice example of a magnetic cloud, which can primarily be seen
by the smooth, rotating B-eld visible in all three components. The Bz value reaches a minimum around
−20 nT, which then gives an estimated Dst minimum of ∼−150 nT. A warning is essential here, since
the event might have severe consequences. The event is also found in Richardson's updated list, where
the real Dst minimum is −122 nT. The estimate is lower than the observed value, but the warning was
right. The list also conrms that it is a MC, by giving the score "2" in the 12th category (MC?). The MC
is bipolar, since the Bz component starts out as negative and then slowly increases to becoming positive
before the event ends. The disturbance is led by a forward shock. It is very clear in this example since the
ambient solar wind is very quiet before. The end of the disturbance shows a more chaotic behaviour, that
explains the wrong categorisation of the remainder of the disturbance in Figure 5.18. In Figure 5.16 the
categorisation becomes more uncertain because the rst part of the ICME leaves the time interval. The
smooth, rotating eld is still dominating, even though the last part has increased uctuation, which means
the category is reduced to ICME-like. This disturbance is also a good example of why continuous obser-
vation and analysis of the solar wind is necessary during the quiet times too. Strong or semi-strong events
might occur suddenly. The long-lasting southwards magnetic eld (∼18 h) makes this event dangerous.
The small disturbance registered as an ICME in Figure 5.18 is not in Richardson's list. The event has
some features like relatively smooth B-components that could indicate a small magnetic cloud is present,
but sine the event is short it is dicult to determine. The categorisation also seems wrong since it is
"unidentied" at the previous extract (Figure 5.17). The major ICME is also found and categorised by
==PAGE:84==

Figure 5.16: A good example of a magnetic cloud with smooth B-components and high Bmax and one
following small disturbance.
Figure 5.17: ICME leaving the time interval and the categorisation becomes more unsure, hence the
category "ICME-like".
AWARE. Here is is categorised as "ICME", which is in agreement with Richardson's and this program's
analysis. The output from AWARE can be seen in Figure 5.19. The Kp index displayed in the lower
part shows a maximum of 6, which indicates a moderate storm. This is interesting since the Dst index
categorises this geomagnetic disturbance as a strong storm.
An other nice example of a MC is from October 2016. It is display in Figure 5.20 and 5.21, at two dierent

becomes unidentied as the beginning is excluded from the analysis, when the time span is moved forward.

for a geomagnetic storm. The MC is unipolar which results in this behaviour. AWARE categorises this

The next example shows that the program does not always categorise the disturbances correctly or nd
the events at all. In the catalogue by Richardson, two ICMEs are registered in the beginning of April 2017.
One at the 4th and one between the 9.-10. April 2017. The plot in Figure 5.22 shows magnetic eld data
from that period. The rst ICME is unidentied because it is not that powerful with Bmax = 15.8 nT and
Bzmin ≈10 nT. Further investigation also suggest that this might be a magnetic cloud, but the smooth
rotation is too disturbed by uctuations to be included by the RMSE and Dierence in smooth and real
data criteria, which means that two of the criteria in the ICME score table are irrelevant. It reduces
the overall score.
The event is categorised as a MC by I. Richardson, which emphasises that manual
investigation is still needed. The estimated Dst index would still decrease below the limit of -50 nT. It
will activate a warning and an manual investigation can follow. AWARE has categorised the disturbance
as "ICME". This shows that plasma data is sometimes necessary to give a proper classication.
From the 9th of April an ICME is registered by I. Richardson, but the program has not detected anything.
As it can be seen in Figure 5.22, the total B remains below 9 nT the entire time. Since the detection
function is the same for AWARE, it does not register the disturbance either. For this disturbance the
Bz component turns positive and no geomagnetic storm is initialised. The estimated Dst turns positive
too. In the catalogue, the Dstmin is -24 nT which is on normal disturbance level. In this case no impacts
is missed, but if the ICME is unipolar with dominating southward direction, a smaller storm could have
been missed. This is also one good example of how a program based on a specic threshold will miss some
events. The two small disturbance are categorised as SIR-like by the program, where AWARE assign them
to the ICME-like category. It is dicult to conclude what is right when using the program and only B-data
is available. The second event beginning at the 8th of April shows some smoothing in the B-component,
which might suggest a MC. It is not possible to conclude more without a manual investigation of the
plasma data and magnetic eld data together.
Figure 5.22: Plot of magnetic eld data with one wrongly categorised (4/4) ICME and one ICME not
detected (9/4-10/4).
==PAGE:88==

Results
Another very interesting example is from September 2017.
It is displayed in Figure 5.23, where is it
categorised as a SIR. The disturbance is special because it is a hybrid event. Just before the start time is
registered, a smaller shock is shown. Then a MC-like disturbance is ongoing. Around midnight between
the 7th and 8th a very strong shock increases the magnitude of B. This strongly indicates that another
event has caught up with the MC. The uctuation after the peak are intense. This can both be a SIR or
a non-MC ICME. The geomagnetic storm resulting from this event is double-peaked. The rst and most
intense decrease in Dst occurs because the decreasing Bz component is intensied by the second shock
pushing the Bz component towards the minimum of -30 nT. The estimated minimum in Dst is lower -200
nT. These indicate a severe geomagnetic storm and require immediate warning signals. The estimated Dst
index is once again overestimated. The value measured at Earth is -124 nT meaning it only resulted in
a strong storm, not severe. The substorms takes place half a day later. The estimated Dst is here -100
nT, which is also critical. The estimated Dst here is similar to the observed value at Earth, which suggest
that the deviations are larger for higher values. The program has categorised this event as a SIR, mainly
based on the strong uctuations after the second shock. Further investigation using iSWA has proven that
it is wrong. iSWA is an online space weather tool, which has collected relevant visual data and models
for space weather investigations [5]. Coronagraph images in Figure 5.24 shows this disturbance is a result
of two ICMEs, the rst is a smaller magnetic cloud and the second is a powerful non-magnetic cloud.
AWARE agrees with the catalogue that it is a ICME. This example is important because it shows how
a weak magnetic cloud can cause severe geomagnetic activity if the Bz component is compressed to very
negative values.
Figure 5.23: Plot of magnetic eld data from September 2017 showing a hybrid event between two ICMEs.
The Kp index for this event also shows a double peak because of the two minimums in Bz.
The
geomagnetic storm level is severe, which can be seen in Figure 5.25. The index Kp index is from iSWA.

be implemented in the next version since it is characteristic for a SIR. The rst event has the classic slow
==PAGE:91==
5.5 Results of simulated real-time investigation
75
Figure 5.28: Disturbance at the 21st of November categorised as unidentied one day later than Figure
5.27.
decreasing B-eld after the SI.
Figure 5.29: Two disturbances categorised as SIR-like by the program. They show high uctuations in the
B-components which well-known from SIRs.
The results of the dierent steps in the process show that the score tables can identify and categorise
a number of events, but the theory-like and extreme cases are easier, since the threshold values can be
more specialised. The trade-o between detecting few, but "true" events compared to detecting as many
as possible has played a major role in the development. The challenged which has become clear during
the process will be discussed in the next chapter, with the purpose of proposing improvements to further
development of the program.
==PAGE:92==
 
==PAGE:93==
6
|
Discussion
The aim of this project is to create an automatic program that solemnly based on magnetic eld data from
the solar wind can detect and categorise disturbances. The risk analysis must then determine the potential
impact of each disturbance. The results presented in chapter 5 have shown that it is possible to detect,
categorise and analyse risks for disturbances in the solar wind for over half of the disturbances. The score
tables make it possible to include several criteria and let likeness features be a part of the categorisation as
three dierent levels of score points are included. The variations in the solar cycle have been proven to be
a signicant factor in the categorisation process and the exact challenges will be elaborated later on in this
chapter. The real-time analysis has been successfully tested. The performance has been considered for the
few events, which have taken place in the end of August 2018 where dierent points of improvements were
highlighted. They were conrmed by the simulated real-time which was a very useful exercise to test the
performance of the real-time code. Otherwise some of the problems might not have been solved because
at this point the solar activity is very low. An important lesson from this project is that over-tting is
a risk when a certain data set is analysed very closely and there is a long term time dependence, which
is dicult to include. It seems that time varying criteria over the solar cycle could improve the detection
rate and the correct classication of events. The results will now be discussed in details and some of the
challenges will be elaborated in order to consider how they might be solved in the future.
AWARE has previously proven that a simple criterion, in this case the increase of the magnitude of B
exceeding 9 nT, can detect almost all disturbances in the solar wind. The challenge in this project is to
nd a way to distinguish dierent types of event only expressed in B. The short story is that it is possible
to build a program with these properties with a decent success rate.
As the evaluation tables have shown, the performance it not perfect yet. For the validation of OMNIweb
data about 50%-65% were hits where they were categorised by the program and matched in the right
catalogue. The percentage of hits will never be 100% since the categorisation method is dierent for the
manual catalogues and the automated programs. Another very important part of the classication is the
false positives, where the disturbances are categorised wrongly. In term of warnings it is very benecial to
know the right category, because as a forecaster you can better predict the impact based on expectations,
but it is not paramount. Even then, the warnings are independent of the category, since it is only based
on the decrease in estimated Dst at the moment. Manual investigation afterwards will conrm the nature
of the disturbances, and more precise predicting and precautions can be issued.
An important issue for the version of the program is the number of unidentied events. For the validation
period almost 40% of the events are unidentied. This must be improved. To comparison, AWARE has
21.59% unidentied events from 1995 to 2017. A result close to this will be a good goal for this future
versions of the program since AWARE is also an automated detection and categorisation program. A com-
plete agreement with AWARE is not deemed to be realistic since the background data is very dierent.
There are dierent methods that could optimize the performance of the program. One challenge, which
became clear when comparing the results from the test and validation period based on the OMNIweb data,
is that the threshold values need to be determined in a more dynamic way. The comparison of Bzmin
values in the two Figures 5.2 and 5.6 show that the general intensity of the disturbances are very dierent.
It is conrmed by Bmax and Bmean comparison in appendix E. By using specic threshold values, either
to many or too little disturbances will be evaluated depending on the time period. The same can be seen
for the Parker spiral angle in Figure 5.3 and 5.7, since the mean angle distribution peaks at a higher value
for the validation. When the evaluation matrix for the test period is considered, the performance of the
program looks really promising with a high number of positive hits, low number of false positives and a
lower ratio of unidentied events. The results from the validation period then suggest that the score tables
==PAGE:94==
78
Discussion
have been over-tted to that exact period between 1998-2008. This also shows the sensitivity to variation
over the course of decades and the solar cycles, since the intensity varies as seen in Figure 2.3 of the solar
cycle.
To meet this problem for the next version of the program, a dynamic way of determining the threshold is
suggested. The strength of this method is that variations in the solar wind and behaviour of the Sun can
be included e.g. by determining the thresholds based on the behaviour of previous categorised events. A
consideration for the future of space weather forecasting is the implementation of machine learning and
neural networks to make the automated categorisation process learn from previous observations and a set
of known features. The idea is that the program can determine the most ecient thresholds within a
limited space of options e.g. that the magnitude of B can only be positive. The perspectives for machine
learning in space weather is mostly unexplored at this point, but it can be a signicant tool in future
programs. It is internationally agreed upon as described in the short summary by E. Camporeale from the
workshop "Space weather: A multidisciplinary Approach" [74]. The possibilities of implementing machine
learning in space weather forecasting must be explored as the general knowledge about the area becomes
more broadly available and the computational power of computers increases. If more time was available,
this could be very interesting to explore. Other methods could be considered too, where the previous data
also will be considered e.g. running mean of dierent criteria thresholds or time specic thresholds to the
minimum and maximum phases. On the other hand it will also make the detection more complicated.
A very important consideration is if the dynamic thresholds increase the performance signicantly or it
just makes the program more complicated and computational heavy. Since the nal version must analyse
real-time data, the computational speed is important too. This would be very interesting to test.
The score tables would still be the preferred categorisation method because it allows for several criteria to
be considered, and it gives the "true" and "likeness" categories which are useful since the features of some
disturbances are a bit unclear. A consideration is if the criteria need to be evaluated once again. The
criteria, sum of RMSE and dierence in smooth and real data are very similar and basically investigate the
same feature. They were both included because they showed a well-dened distinction between ICMEs and
SIRs, but disturbances with smooth rotating eld will typically get a high score in both criteria anyway.
The RMSE and dierence criteria are both targeting MCs in the ICME score table, which are important,
but they only account for about 30 % of the observed ICMEs over the course of one solar cycle [7]. This
means the non-MC ICMEs have a diculty being detected as ICMEs because their features in some way
are very close to SIRs in magnetic eld data. This was also seen for the ICME observed on the 25/8
2018, that is wrongly categorised as a SIR. A way to comply with this problem is to create a subcategory
more called MC or a separate score table for MCs. Once again a solution including this must be tested
if it increases the performance signicantly or it just complicates the process. Some criteria for example
are general for ICMEs (non-MC and MCs) such as very negative Bz, high peak in B, peak of B close to
the start of the event, and shocks. This is one of the focus point for further work since it can improve
the number of hits, minimise the number of false positive, and likely reduce the number of unidentied
disturbances at the same time.
One type of events which will always be a challenge is the hybrid events like the ones seen in Figure 5.23
and 5.26. If the disturbance is a mixture between an ICME and a SIR, the aim must be to categorise it as
the most dominant event. At the moment the hybrid events are categorised as SIR/SIR-like because they
usually show large chaotic behaviour. The magnetic cloud signatures are often obscured when another
ICME or SIR interferes because the magnetic eld is then compressed. It leads to wrong categorisation
as seen in Figure 5.23. It might also be interesting to consider if the RMSE and dierence in smooth and
real data criteria should work on the whole event if the peak of B is close to the end. This might help
detect some of the MCs that are caught by another disturbance or do not have the shock front. The time
interval should then depend on the time position of Bmax. Better non-MC ICME categorisation could also
improve this performance, because more ICMEs would be detected. Another thing, which might improve
this, could be to make the program match it results to iSWA, where LASCO observations and X-ray images
of the Sun are available. They could conrm if a CME is seen a few days before or if the solar atmosphere
is dominated by a coronal hole. The analysis could also be used as precursor for potential impact if a CME
or coronal hole is observed. This implementation is complicated, since it combines a dierent types of
analysis, but the perspectives are really interesting. Space weather is truly a complicated system. Obser-
vations from many sources could contributed to better understanding and forecasting. Existing programs
also build on automatism could supplement this analysis [41].
An attempt to include CIRs as a category was part of the development phase. The code section was based
on checking the time interval between disturbances categorised as SIR/SIR-like, and then selecting out the
==PAGE:95==
79
pair separated by 25-29 days. As a second requirement the mean polarity of Bx and By have to be the
same in both disturbances, since CIRs originates from the same coronal hole. Several problems occur when
the results are analysed. Firstly the number of CIRs detected compared to the number of SIR/SIR-like
disturbances is too low. One argument can be that not all of SIRs are detected which means that the
basis of the process is wrong. This can be true to some extend, but the ratio of CIR to the total number
of interaction region categorisations is still expected to be larger than 18.2 %. Another indication that
the detection process is insucient, is that equally many of the CIRs are found in the Jian catalogue as
the Richardson and Cane catalogue. A challenge with detection CIRs in only magnetic eld data is that
magnetic eld conguration can chance a lot over one orbit. It is possible that magnetic eld data does
not include enough stable parameters to detect CIRs as the pressure prole normally is the signicant
feature. An improvement of the SIR score table will likely have positive impact on the CIR categorisation
and therefore it is to soon to exclude it completely. Other signatures might also be considered for better
conrming if it is the same SIR co-rotating with the Parker spiral.
An interesting study was carried out by Susanne Vennerstrøm et. al. [16], where the development of
CIRs in the quiet and active period of the solar cycle through several solar rotations, was examined. Data
from STEA, STEB and OMNIweb was included in the study. It can be seen in Figure 6.1 and 6.2, where
dierent CIRs are followed over a couple of months. In year 2008 the CIR is easy to follow since the
ambient solar wind is quiet, but variations in the magnetic eld are still visible. The length of the CIR
changes, even between the three spacecraft. The prole of the magnetic eld also changes between the
spacecraft, which challenges the detection only based on B data. Even though there are variations, it
should be possible to detect CIR through the quiet periods. In active periods the detection will be more
uncertain. In 2012, where the Sun is very active, the CIR is far more dicult to follow. It is partly
caused by ICMEs (light blue boxes) interfering with the CIRs (dark blue boxes). The overall magnetic
structure is even more chaotic here. The example from the beginning of 2012 also shows the challenge
of dierent types of disturbances merging. The CIR might be categorised as a SIR/SIR-like event during
the rst rotation and as ICME/ICME-like in the next. Then the CIR would not be detected, when only
SIR/SIR-like disturbances are considered.
Figure 6.1: Following a CIR through STEB, OMNIweb and STEA during a quiet period in 2008 [16].
A central part of the project is also to estimate the risk of incoming disturbances. The risk can be un-
derstood in several ways, but the focus here is to determine the chance of geomagnetic storms and the
estimated strength of those. The chosen method is simple and ecient. The linear relation between the
Bzmin and the Dstmin serves as an easy way to estimate the strength of a possible geomagnetic storm.
==PAGE:96==
80
Discussion
Figure 6.2: Following a CIR though STEB, OMNIweb and STEA in an active period in 2012.
The correlation is well-dened in the literature as it is commonly understood that negative Bz is the main
driver of geomagnetic storms. The relation is investigated in several articles as seen in table 2.5. The
relation was also tested in the program, rst for all disturbances and then only for those categorised as
ICME/ICME-like. The correlation coecient was highest for the latter, which of course challenges the
decision of using it for all disturbances in the output. The decision has been to accept this deviation
because the correlation coecient still is 0.63 for all events, and the estimated Dst only served as indicator
for necessary warning signals. This is a point which can be developed further in the future. It could also be
relevant to nd a relation between the magnetic eld data and Kp, since Kp can be used as an additional
indicator for impact strength. Kp is also known to describe the impacts happening at the high latitudes
better than Dst. Another possibility which could be interesting for the real-time detections is to extract
the Dst data directly from the WDC for geomagnetism webpage, where real-time data is available with a
delay of 2 hours. An option is to estimate the Dst index with the linear relation and then when the Dst
index is available allow it to substitute the estimated value. The estimated value has also been observed to
overestimate the Dst index, which could lead to false alarms. It is a trade-o, which is preferred compared
to not knowing if a critical situation is ongoing. As an additional study, it would be interesting to know
how many false alarms would be issued. The estimated Dst is calculated as soon as the Bz component is
known. It means that it is a few hours ahead of the real impact. Forecasters can use this method to see
when a storm is coming and then further investigation can be carried out manually.
If the warning system should be developed, the time interval in which the Dst index is below -50 nT should
also be apart of the risk estimation. It has been observed that long-lasting negative Bz condition can drive
really intense storms. Shocks could also be included as indicators of fast ICMEs and SIRs coming.
The geoeectiveness is also considered. The disturbances categorised by the program have a higher geoef-
fectiveness than those from the catalogues, which is expected since the program is designed for prioritising
detection of geoecient disturbances. The program also categorises intense events better, which usually
also have a higher change of driving geomagnetic storm. The geoeectiveness is a test, which can be used
for future test to see that the ratio is still high.
The OMNIweb data shows mixed results for the test and validation period, but the behaviour is possible
to explain when the results and data is examined. This is much harder for the STEREO A and B data.
For both spacecraft the detection rate of SIR and SIR-like disturbances is very low, especially compared
to the detection rate of the ICME-like disturbances. It is surprising since the validation period overlap
with the validation period for OMNIweb data, where the tendency is opposite. It has not been possible to
==PAGE:97==
81
nd a explanation for this during the project. In the investigation of the development of the disturbance
through STEB, L1 and STEA it is clear to see that both STEREO data sets behave strange. One curious
discovery is that the general intensity of the data seems increased for the STEREO data, e.g. the number
of disturbances detected is higher. The number is similar to that in the catalogues which supports that
it is correct.
Most of the categorised disturbances are also categorised as ICME-like, where most are
categorised as SIR-like for the OMNIweb data. 2008, where the investigation is done, is close to minimum
between the solar cycle 23 and 24, which means that the number of SIRs is high and only few ICMEs are
expected. This supports the idea that the STEREO categorisations are wrong.
The last investigation highlights some of the challenges concerning predicting SIRs/CIRs by using double
spacecraft observations. One major issue is that the IMF is very dynamic and disturbances can disappear,
merge, and split up. In a study by L. Jian et. al. of solar wind observations at STEREO a & B, it is also
concluded that the number of observed events is not the same [53]. A similar investigation of observations
in STEB, L1, and STEA conrmed the dynamic nature [53]. It is possible to follow the development of
some of the more dominant disturbances. The idea is good, but the recommendation is to test the method
with both plasma and IMf data to develop a forecasting method.
The primary focus in this project has been to develop and improve the detection and categorisation. This
means that the program can be improved to work more smoothly and even more automatised with high
computational speed. The problem is most relevant when large sets of data are analysed for the long time
spans. When the real time data is analysed, the processing time is not a big problem, since the data
amount is limited. If more data where to be included from e.g. iSWA and the online Dst index, then
optimisation is relevant.
At the moment, the real time program works with 7 days at a time. To compare, AWARE analyses three
days at a time. Both solutions have benets. The data amount is less for 3 days, which is considerable.
On the other hand some disturbances have been close to three days long, and then the whole disturbance
is not visible together. Since this program only uses IMF data the 7 days is an accepted time interval. It
is also directly available online.
One issue which turned out to be relevant when the simulated real-time is examined, is the stability of
the categorisation of a disturbance. As seen en the Figures 5.27 and 5.28 the categorisation changes even
though the disturbance is unchanged. This suggests that the stability of the categorisation needs to be
considered.
The uncertainty when a disturbance enters and leave the time interval is expected.
The
changes in the middle is more of a mystery. It would be interesting to follow the behaviour of specic
criteria to see what changes and then be able to prevent this. Some of the criteria depends on time periods
before the start time, which might explain some of the changes.
This exact process based on only using magnetic eld data for disturbance analysis has not been conducted
before, which made the results very interesting. The criteria are based on the literature where the sum
of RMSE and dierence between smooth and real data both are based on the characteristic feature of
the MC magnetic eld structure. It proved to be very useful to characterise this type of events. The
same is true for categorising SIRs where the large uctuations are dominating. What might be interesting
to investigate in a manually conducted study, is the dierence in magnetic eld signatures between the
non-MC ICMEs and the powerful SIRs. Here a large overlap is seen, which made the detection of non-MCs
challenging. The shock detection in the project was based on some very simple criteria. It worked, but
the process might be improved by looking at other automatised programs [39, 38]. It was not done in this
project due to time limitations. The implementation also needs to adjusted to solemnly B-eld data. It
was interesting to compare the categorisation of the program to AWARE, since there are some similarities
in the process. Generally AWARE had a better success rate, but ≈20% of all events between 1995-2017
are still categorised as "Unidentied". This level of categorisation should be possible to achieve when the
improvements presented above are implemented. At this point AWARE is better, which means that the
full amount of parameters improve the performance. This is of course expected, but compared to only
having one parameter, it is impressive how much information can be extracted and analysed.
Within the area of space weather forecasting this program can serves as a complement to AWARE and
other real-time analysis programs. The benet of this is that it only depends in magnetic eld data to
analyse incoming disturbances, which makes it more robust when extreme conditions are present. The risk
analysis is simple, but it can give a warning a few hours before the geomagnetic storm actually inuences
the atmosphere, which give a longer warning time. It can also estimate the intensity of the coming storm.
This is essential for protection of sensitive equipment and initialisation of emergency procedures e.g. in
power grid or GNSS based technologies, where geomagnetic storms are known to cause problems.
==PAGE:98==
 
==PAGE:99==
7
|
Future work
As addressed in the discussion in chapter 6, there are several improvements, which are relevant for the
further development of the program.
At rst another way of determining the threshold values for the score tables must be included. Machine
learning could be a possible approach, but it would also require extensive research of the relevant methods
and models in the eld of research. Neural networks are another possible approach, as these are particu-
larly capable of recognizing patterns in complex, unstructured data. Other more simple ways could also
be considered such as running mean or time depended values.
The next challenge which must be addressed is the stability of the categorisation. This could involve
re-evaluation of some of the criteria or determine a certain point at which the disturbance is assigned to
a category. Several solutions must be considered for this.
Next, the estimation of the risks of each impact could be further developed, such as included the real time
Dst index distributed by WDC for Geomagnetism and determine/nd a relation between the Kp index and
magnetic eld data to supplement the Dst index. The risk estimation should also be considered in relation
to the time span of the impact. Shock could also contribute as an early warning signal since shocks are
known to be associated with geomagnetic disturbances [39, 37].
Additionally, it would be benecial to develop another method for detecting CIRs. They are interesting
phenomena, and should be included for the program to address the most well-known space weather phe-
nomena. It is expected to be easier to categorise CIRs properly when the above-mentioned improvements
are implemented. A manual investigation similar to the one shown in Figure 6.1 and 6.2 could provide
more features to categorise after. Shock could be used as a feature since more CIRs than SIRs are known
to have shocks [8].
Lastly, when all the above-mentioned improvements are implemented and the program is deemed reliable
enough, the program could be made available as an online tool for space weather analysis like AWARE is
now. It could then be implemented in commercial applications where warnings are necessary.
==PAGE:100==
 
==PAGE:101==
8
|
Conclusion
In conclusion, this project has led to the development of a Matlab program, which can open, analyse, visu-
alise and save relevant information about disturbances found in the magnetic eld data from DSCOVR in
real-time. The analysis was developed using OMNIweb data, where the most important features of ICMEs
and SIRs where implemented.
The chosen detection method was two separate score tables for ICMEs and SIRs, which assigned every
disturbance to a score based on three levels of thresholds values and their respective score point from 1-3.
The thresholds were based on analyses of events from the ICME catalogue by I. Richardson and H. Cane
and the SIR catalogue by L. Jian, which were chosen as references for the categorisation. In the nal
version, ve dierent categorises were included: ICME, ICME-like, SIR, SIR-like, and unidentied.
The nal version of the score tables categorised 70 % of the disturbances detected by the program in the
test period from 1998 to 2008, with a high geoeectiveness among the ICME/ICME-like events and the
SIR/SIR-like events. The validation based on the OMNIweb data between 2009-2014 showed that the
thresholds had been over-tted to the test period, and highlighted the most important issue for improve-
ments. The threshold values must be determined in a more dynamic way which takes the variations during
one solar cycle and the change in intensity between each cycle into account.
The validation of the STEREO A and B data between 2007-2012 showed diverging behaviour with false
categorisation of most events. The majority of the events where expected to be SIR/SIR-like events, as
seen in the OMNIweb validation. Further investigation also concluded that the STEREO validation has
been inicted with a dominating error and the results are therefore wrong. The manual investigation of the
development of disturbances between STEREO B, L1 and STEREO A conrmed the dynamic behaviour
of the IMF, which will complicate early forecasting by satellites located upstream from Earth.
After the development phase, real-time data from DSCOVR was implemented and proved that the pro-
gram can detect, categorise and estimate the risk of geomagnetic storm, based on a simple linear relation
between Bzmin and Dstmin. The linear relation had a tendency to overestimate the Dstmin, but it served
as a good indication for needed warnings. For future development it is considered to use the real-time Dst
index from WDC for geomagnetism available online with a 2 hours delay. The duration of the negative Bz
interval as well as detected shocks can also be included in the risk assessment for future versions.
The simulated real-time investigation showed that the stability of a categorisation of a disturbance must
be improved, since examples showed that the category changed. The investigation also conrmed that the
categorisation is uncertain when the disturbance enters or leaves the time interval of interest.
Further work must also improve the categorisation of non-MC ICMEs, since the current version targets
MC mostly. It was discovered that the magnetic eld features of SIRs and non-MC ICMEs are similar
which led to examples of wrong categorisation. It was e.g. seen in hybrid events, which usually include
larger uctuations in the B components. The possibilities of CIR detection must also be examined further
and a new approach attempted in order to include it in the analysis and categorisation.
The aim of this project was to build a program that could characterise potential geo-eective event near
Earth and estimate the risk, solemnly based on magnetic eld data, which is successfully done. More
than half of the disturbances in the validation process was categorised and the risk assessment is simple,
but very ecient since it is coupled to the magnetic eld observations. The real-time data is analysed
continuously, and the score tables function as intended. The mentioned improvements are expected to im-
prove the performance even more. This thesis project contributes to the eld of space weather forecasting
by proving that it is possible to categorise disturbances in the solar wind only using magnetic eld data.
This gives a solution which is robust to extreme space weather conditions which can limit the access to
plasma data during longer periods of time. The continuous real-time analysis can supplement programs
such as AWARE in predicting potential dangerous impacts and thereby try to minimise the complicated
consequences in society, when a geomagnetic storm hits.
